<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Achaemenes" title="Achaemenes">Achaemenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teispids" title="Teispids">Teispids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_family_tree" title="Achaemenid family tree">Achaemenid family tree</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_lineage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1511" data-file-width="1083" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Achaemenid_lineage.jpg/220px-Achaemenid_lineage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Achaemenid_lineage.jpg/330px-Achaemenid_lineage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Achaemenid_lineage.jpg/440px-Achaemenid_lineage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_lineage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family tree of the Achaemenid rulers.</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Persian nation contains a number of tribes as listed here. ... : the Pasargadae, <a href="/wiki/Maraphii" title="Maraphii">Maraphii</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maspii" title="Maspii">Maspii</a>, upon which all the other tribes are dependent. Of these, the Pasargadae are the most distinguished; they contain the clan of the Achaemenids from which spring the Perseid kings. Other tribes are the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Panthialaei&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Panthialaei (page does not exist)">Panthialaei</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Derusiaei&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Derusiaei (page does not exist)">Derusiaei</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kerman_Province#History_and_culture" title="Kerman Province">Germanii</a>, all of which are attached to the soil, the remainder—the <a href="/wiki/Dahae" title="Dahae">Dai</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mardi_(people)" title="Mardi (people)">Mardi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dropici" title="Dropici">Dropici</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sagarti" title="Sagarti">Sagarti</a>, being <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a>.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">Histories</a> 1.101 &amp; 125</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>The Achaemenid Empire was created by nomadic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a>. The Persians were <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian people</a> who arrived in what is today <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> c. 1000 BC and settled a region including north-western Iran, the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persis" title="Persis">Persis</a> alongside the native <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elamites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrosius20063_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrosius20063-31">[28]</a></sup> The Persians were originally <a href="/wiki/Nomadic_pastoralism" title="Nomadic pastoralism">nomadic pastoralists</a> in the western Iranian Plateau. The Achaemenid Empire may not have been the first Iranian empire, as the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, another group of Iranian people, possibly established a short-lived empire when they played a major role in overthrowing of the Assyrians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Achaemenids were initially rulers of the Elamite city of <a href="/wiki/Anshan_(Persia)" title="Anshan (Persia)">Anshan</a> near the modern city of <a href="/wiki/Marvdasht" title="Marvdasht">Marvdasht</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200217_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200217-33">[30]</a></sup> the title "King of Anshan" was an adaptation of the earlier Elamite title "King of Susa and Anshan".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrosius20066_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrosius20066-34">[31]</a></sup> There are conflicting accounts of the identities of the earliest Kings of Anshan. According to the <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_Cylinder" title="Cyrus Cylinder">Cyrus Cylinder</a> (the oldest extant genealogy of the Achaemenids) the kings of Anshan were <a href="/wiki/Teispes" title="Teispes">Teispes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_I" title="Cyrus I">Cyrus I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_I" title="Cambyses I">Cambyses I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus II</a>, also known as Cyrus the Great, who created the empire<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200217_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200217-33">[30]</a></sup> (the later <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a>, written by <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a>, claims that Teispes was the son of <a href="/wiki/Achaemenes" title="Achaemenes">Achaemenes</a> and that Darius is also descended from Teispes through a different line, but no earlier texts mention Achaemenes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200216_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200216-35">[32]</a></sup>). In <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>' <a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">Histories</a>, he writes that Cyrus the Great was the son of Cambyses I and <a href="/wiki/Mandane_of_Media" title="Mandane of Media">Mandane of Media</a>, the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Astyages" title="Astyages">Astyages</a>, the king of the Median Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200215_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200215-36">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_expansion">Formation and expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Formation and expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wars_of_Cyrus_the_Great" title="Wars of Cyrus the Great">Wars of Cyrus the Great</a>, <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Achaemenid conquest of Egypt">Achaemenid conquest of Egypt</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid_invasion_of_the_Indus_Valley" title="Achaemenid invasion of the Indus Valley">Achaemenid invasion of the Indus Valley</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Scythian_campaign_of_Darius_I" title="European Scythian campaign of Darius I">European Scythian campaign of Darius I</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_(flat_map).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="442" data-file-width="672" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg/330px-Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg/495px-Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg/660px-Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_(flat_map).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the expansion process of Achaemenid territories</div></div></div>
<p>Cyrus revolted against the Median Empire in 553 BC, and in 550 BC succeeded in defeating the Medes, capturing Astyages and taking the Median capital city of <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200231_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200231-39">[36]</a></sup> Once in control of Ecbatana, Cyrus styled himself as the successor to Astyages and assumed control of the entire empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200233_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200233-40">[37]</a></sup> By inheriting Astyages' empire, he also inherited the territorial conflicts the Medes had had with both <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200234_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200234-41">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>King <a href="/wiki/Croesus" title="Croesus">Croesus</a> of Lydia sought to take advantage of the new international situation by advancing into what had previously been Median territory in Asia Minor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200235_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200235-43">[40]</a></sup> Cyrus led a counterattack which not only fought off Croesus' armies, but also led to the capture of <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a> and the fall of the Lydian Kingdom in 546 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200236_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200236-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrosius200611_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrosius200611-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[d]</a></sup> Cyrus placed <a href="/wiki/Pactyes" title="Pactyes">Pactyes</a> in charge of collecting tribute in Lydia and left, but once Cyrus had left Pactyes instigated a rebellion against Cyrus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrosius200611_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrosius200611-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200237_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200237-47">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[44]</a></sup> Cyrus sent the Median general <a href="/wiki/Mazares" title="Mazares">Mazares</a> to deal with the rebellion, and Pactyes was captured. Mazares, and after his death <a href="/wiki/Harpagus" title="Harpagus">Harpagus</a>, set about reducing all the cities which had taken part in the rebellion. The subjugation of Lydia took about four years in total.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200237–38_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200237–38-49">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the power in Ecbatana changed hands from the Medes to the Persians, many tributaries to the Median Empire believed their situation had changed and revolted against Cyrus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup> This forced Cyrus to fight wars against <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> and the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a> in Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200239_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200239-51">[47]</a></sup> During these wars, Cyrus established several garrison towns in Central Asia, including the <a href="/wiki/Cyropolis" title="Cyropolis">Cyropolis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200240_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200240-52">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="507" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg/170px-Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg/255px-Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg/340px-Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> is said, in the Bible, to have liberated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_captivity" title="Babylonian captivity">captives in Babylon</a> to resettle and rebuild <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, earning him an honored place in Judaism.</div></div></div>
<p>Nothing is known of Persian-Babylonian relations between 547 BC and 539 BC, but it is likely that there were hostilities between the two empires for several years leading up to the war of 540–539 BC and the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Babylon" title="Fall of Babylon">Fall of Babylon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200241–43_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200241–43-53">[49]</a></sup> In October 539 BC, Cyrus won a battle against the Babylonians at <a href="/wiki/Opis" title="Opis">Opis</a>, then took <a href="/wiki/Sippar" title="Sippar">Sippar</a> without a fight before finally capturing the city of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> on 12 October, where the Babylonian king <a href="/wiki/Nabonidus" title="Nabonidus">Nabonidus</a> was taken prisoner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200241–43_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200241–43-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrosius200611–12_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrosius200611–12-55">[51]</a></sup> Upon taking control of the city, Cyrus depicted himself in propaganda as restoring the divine order which had been disrupted by Nabonidus, who had promoted the cult of <a href="/wiki/Sin_(mythology)" title="Sin (mythology)">Sin</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuhrt198385–86_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuhrt198385–86-57">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200243–44_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200243–44-58">[54]</a></sup> and he also portrayed himself as restoring the heritage of the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> by comparing himself to the Assyrian king <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">Ashurbanipal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuhrt198388–89_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuhrt198388–89-60">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200243–44_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200243–44-58">[54]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> also unreservedly praises Cyrus for his actions in the conquest of Babylon, referring to him as <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">Yahweh</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">anointed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200246_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200246-61">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup> He is credited with freeing the people of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yehud_(Province)" title="Yehud (Province)">Judah</a> from their exile and with authorizing the reconstruction of much of <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple" title="Second Temple">Second Temple</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200246_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200246-61">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pasargad_Tomb_Cyrus3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="5996" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pasargad_Tomb_Cyrus3.jpg/220px-Pasargad_Tomb_Cyrus3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pasargad_Tomb_Cyrus3.jpg/330px-Pasargad_Tomb_Cyrus3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pasargad_Tomb_Cyrus3.jpg/440px-Pasargad_Tomb_Cyrus3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pasargad_Tomb_Cyrus3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Cyrus" title="Tomb of Cyrus">tomb of Cyrus the Great</a>, founder of the Achaemenid Empire. At Pasargadae, Iran.</div></div></div>
<p>In 530 BC, Cyrus presumably died while on a military expedition against the <a href="/wiki/Massagetae" title="Massagetae">Massagetae</a> in Central Asia. He was succeeded by his eldest son <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a>, while his younger son <a href="/wiki/Bardiya" title="Bardiya">Bardiya</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bardiya_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-bardiya-66">[e]</a></sup> received a large territory in Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200249–50_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200249–50-67">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrosius200613_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrosius200613-68">[63]</a></sup> By 525 BC, Cambyses had successfully subjugated <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and was making preparations to invade <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> with the newly created Persian navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallinga1984406–409_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallinga1984406–409-69">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55-70">[65]</a></sup> The great Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Amasis_II" title="Amasis II">Amasis II</a> had died in 526 BC and had been succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Psamtik_III" title="Psamtik III">Psamtik III</a>, resulting in the defection of key Egyptian allies to the Persians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55-70">[65]</a></sup> Psamtik positioned his army at <a href="/wiki/Pelusium" title="Pelusium">Pelusium</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a>. He was soundly defeated by the Persians in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pelusium" title="Battle of Pelusium">Battle of Pelusium</a> before fleeing to <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a>, where the Persians defeated him and took him prisoner. After attempting a failed revolt, Psamtik III promptly committed suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55-70">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-III.11,13_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-III.11,13-71">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Herodotus depicts Cambyses as openly antagonistic to the Egyptian people and their gods, cults, temples, and priests, in particular stressing the murder of the sacred bull <a href="/wiki/Apis_(deity)" title="Apis (deity)">Apis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup> He says that these actions led to a madness that caused him to kill his brother Bardiya (who Herodotus says was killed in secret),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[68]</a></sup> his own sister-wife<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup> and Croesus of Lydia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup> He then concludes that Cambyses completely lost his mind,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[71]</a></sup> and all later classical authors repeat the themes of Cambyses' impiety and madness. However, this is based on spurious information, as the epitaph of Apis from 524 BC shows that Cambyses participated in the funeral rites of Apis styling himself as pharaoh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200255–57_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200255–57-77">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_in_500_BCE.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1986" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/World_in_500_BCE.png/250px-World_in_500_BCE.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/World_in_500_BCE.png/375px-World_in_500_BCE.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/World_in_500_BCE.png/500px-World_in_500_BCE.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_in_500_BCE.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview map of the world in 500 BC</div></div></div>
<p>Following the conquest of Egypt, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libyans</a> and the Greeks of <a href="/wiki/Cyrene,_Libya" title="Cyrene, Libya">Cyrene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barca_(ancient_city)" title="Barca (ancient city)">Barca</a> in present-day eastern Libya (<a href="/wiki/Cyrenaica" title="Cyrenaica">Cyrenaica</a>) surrendered to Cambyses and sent tribute without a fight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55-70">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-III.11,13_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-III.11,13-71">[66]</a></sup> Cambyses then planned invasions of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>, the oasis of Ammon and <a href="/wiki/Aethiopia" title="Aethiopia">Ethiopia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup> Herodotus claims that the naval invasion of Carthage was canceled because the Phoenicians, who made up a large part of Cambyses' fleet, refused to take up arms against their own people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup> but modern historians doubt whether an invasion of Carthage was ever planned at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55_70-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55-70">[65]</a></sup> However, Cambyses dedicated his efforts to the other two campaigns, aiming to improve the Empire's strategic position in Africa by conquering the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kingdom of Meroë</a> and taking strategic positions in the western oases. To this end, he established a garrison at <a href="/wiki/Elephantine" title="Elephantine">Elephantine</a> consisting mainly of Jewish soldiers, who remained stationed at Elephantine throughout Cambyses' reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55_70-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55-70">[65]</a></sup> The invasions of Ammon and Ethiopia themselves were failures. Herodotus claims that the invasion of Ethiopia was a failure due to the madness of Cambyses and the lack of supplies for his men,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup> but archaeological evidence suggests that the expedition was not a failure, and a fortress at the <a href="/wiki/Cataracts_of_the_Nile" title="Cataracts of the Nile">Second Cataract of the Nile</a>, on the border between Egypt and Kush, remained in use throughout the Achaemenid period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55_70-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200252–55-70">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeidorn1992147–150_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeidorn1992147–150-81">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The events surrounding Cambyses' death and Bardiya's succession are greatly debated as there are many conflicting accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200298_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200298-65">[61]</a></sup> According to Herodotus, as Bardiya's assassination had been committed in secret, the majority of Persians still believed him to be alive. This allowed two <a href="/wiki/Magi" title="Magi">Magi</a> to rise up against Cambyses, with one of them sitting on the throne able to impersonate Bardiya because of their remarkable physical resemblance and shared name (Smerdis in Herodotus' accounts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bardiya_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-bardiya-66">[e]</a></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ctesias" title="Ctesias">Ctesias</a> writes that when Cambyses had Bardiya killed he immediately put the magus Sphendadates in his place as satrap of Bactria due to a remarkable physical resemblance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[78]</a></sup> Two of Cambyses' confidants then conspired to usurp Cambyses and put Sphendadates on the throne under the guise of Bardiya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[79]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a>, written by the following king <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a>, a magus named Gaumata impersonated Bardiya and incited a revolution in Persia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-behistunsmerdis_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-behistunsmerdis-64">[60]</a></sup> Whatever the exact circumstances of the revolt, Cambyses heard news of it in the summer of 522 BC and began to return from Egypt, but he was wounded in the thigh in Syria and died of gangrene, so Bardiya's impersonator became king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200261_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200261-85">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[f]</a></sup> The account of Darius is the earliest, and although the later historians all agree on the key details of the story, that a magus impersonated Bardiya and took the throne, this may have been a story created by Darius to justify his own usurpation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant2002100–101_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant2002100–101-88">[82]</a></sup> Iranologist <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Briant" title="Pierre Briant">Pierre Briant</a> hypothesises that Bardiya was not killed by Cambyses, but waited until his death in the summer of 522 BC to claim his legitimate right to the throne as he was then the only male descendant of the royal family. Briant says that although the hypothesis of a deception by Darius is generally accepted today, "nothing has been established with certainty at the present time, given the available evidence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant2002101–103_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant2002101–103-89">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg/250px-Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg/375px-Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg/500px-Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Achaemenid Empire at its greatest extent, c. 500 BC</div></div></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a>, Gaumata ruled for seven months before being overthrown in 522 BC by <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> (Darius I) (Old Persian <i>Dāryavuš</i>, "who holds firm the good", also known as <i>Darayarahush</i>). The Magi, though persecuted, continued to exist, and a year following the death of the first pseudo-Smerdis (Gaumata), saw a second pseudo-Smerdis (named Vahyazdāta) attempt a coup. The coup, though initially successful, failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> writes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[85]</a></sup> that the native leadership debated the best form of government for the empire.
</p><p>Ever since the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonians" title="Ancient Macedonians">Macedonian</a> king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amyntas_I" title="Amyntas I">Amyntas I</a> surrendered his country to the Persians in about 512–511, Macedonians and Persians were strangers no more as well. The subjugation of <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonia</a> was part of Persian military operations initiated by <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> (521–486) in 513—after immense preparations—a huge Achaemenid army invaded the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Scythian_campaign_of_Darius_I" title="European Scythian campaign of Darius I">tried to defeat</a> the European <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> roaming to the north of the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-92">[86]</a></sup> Darius' army subjugated several <a href="/wiki/Thracians" title="Thracians">Thracian peoples</a>, and virtually all other regions that touch the European part of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, such as parts of nowadays <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, before it returned to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-92">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup> Darius left in Europe one of his commanders named <a href="/wiki/Megabazus" title="Megabazus">Megabazus</a> whose task was to accomplish conquests in the Balkans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-92">[86]</a></sup> The Persian troops subjugated gold-rich <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, the coastal Greek cities, and defeated and conquered the powerful <a href="/wiki/Paeonians" title="Paeonians">Paeonians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-92">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoweReames2008239_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoweReames2008239-95">[89]</a></sup> Finally, Megabazus sent envoys to Amyntas, demanding acceptance of Persian domination, which the Macedonians did. The Balkans provided many soldiers for the multi-ethnic Achaemenid army. Many of the Macedonian and Persian elite intermarried, such as the Persian official <a href="/wiki/Bubares" title="Bubares">Bubares</a> who married Amyntas' daughter, Gygaea. Family ties the Macedonian rulers Amyntas and Alexander enjoyed with Bubares ensured them good relations with the Persian kings Darius and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xerxes_the_Great" title="Xerxes the Great">Xerxes the Great</a>. The Persian invasion led indirectly to Macedonia's rise in power and Persia had some common interests in the Balkans; with Persian aid, the Macedonians stood to gain much at the expense of some Balkan tribes such as the Paeonians and Greeks. All in all, the Macedonians were "willing and useful Persian allies. Macedonian soldiers fought against Athens and <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a> in Xerxes the Great's army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_92-4"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-92">[86]</a></sup> The Persians referred to both Greeks and Macedonians as <i><a href="/wiki/Yona" title="Yona">Yauna</a></i> ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionian_Greeks" title="Ionian Greeks">Ionians</a>", their term for "Greeks"), and to Macedonians specifically as <i>Yaunã Takabara</i> or "Greeks with hats that look like shields", possibly referring to the Macedonian <a href="/wiki/Kausia" title="Kausia">kausia</a> hat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_prince%27s_head_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Achaemenid_prince%27s_head_2.jpg/170px-Achaemenid_prince%27s_head_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Achaemenid_prince%27s_head_2.jpg/255px-Achaemenid_prince%27s_head_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Achaemenid_prince%27s_head_2.jpg/340px-Achaemenid_prince%27s_head_2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_prince%27s_head_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Persian queen <a href="/wiki/Atossa" title="Atossa">Atossa</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>, sister-wife of <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a>'s wife, and mother of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xerxes_the_Great" title="Xerxes the Great">Xerxes the Great</a></div></div></div>
<p>By the 5th century BC, the Kings of Persia were either ruling over or had subordinated territories encompassing not just all of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Plateau" title="Persian Plateau">Persian Plateau</a> and all of the territories formerly held by the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyrian Empire</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Egypt" title="Persian Egypt">Egypt</a>), but beyond this all of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Caucasus" title="Southern Caucasus">Southern Caucasus</a> and parts of the <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">North Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paeonia_(kingdom)" title="Paeonia (kingdom)">Paeonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonia</a> to the north and west, most of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> coastal regions, parts of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> as far as the <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Amu_Darya" title="Amu Darya">Oxus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Jaxartes</a> to the north and north-east, the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> and the western <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus basin</a> (corresponding to modern Afghanistan and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>) to the far east, parts of northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> to the south, and parts of eastern <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libya</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cyrenaica" title="Cyrenaica">Cyrenaica</a>) to the south-west, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>, China, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UAE" title="UAE">UAE</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius.org_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-livius.org-97">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-behi_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-behi-98">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c_ramirez-faria_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-c_ramirez-faria-101">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuhrt20132_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuhrt20132-102">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greco-Persian_Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Greco-Persian Wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="793" data-file-width="992" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/280px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/420px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/560px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing events of the first phases of the Greco-Persian Wars</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek-Persian_duel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="859" data-file-width="868" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Greek-Persian_duel.jpg/220px-Greek-Persian_duel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Greek-Persian_duel.jpg/330px-Greek-Persian_duel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Greek-Persian_duel.jpg/440px-Greek-Persian_duel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek-Persian_duel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greek <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">hoplite</a> and Persian warrior depicted fighting, on an ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kylix_(drinking_cup)" title="Kylix (drinking cup)">kylix</a>, 5th century BC</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a> in 499 BC, and associated revolts in Aeolis, Doris, Cyprus, and Caria, were military rebellions by several regions of Asia Minor against Persian rule, lasting from 499 to 493 BC. At the heart of the rebellion was the dissatisfaction of the Greek cities of Asia Minor with the tyrants appointed by Persia to rule them, along with the individual actions of two Milesian tyrants, <a href="/wiki/Histiaeus" title="Histiaeus">Histiaeus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristagoras" title="Aristagoras">Aristagoras</a>. In 499 BC, the then-tyrant of <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">Miletus</a>, Aristagoras, launched a joint expedition with the Persian satrap Artaphernes to conquer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naxos_(island)" title="Naxos (island)">Naxos</a>, in an attempt to bolster his position in Miletus (both financially and in terms of prestige). The mission was a debacle, and sensing his imminent removal as tyrant, Aristagoras chose to incite the whole of Ionia into rebellion against the Persian king Darius the Great.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Persians continued to reduce the cities along the west coast that still held out against them, before finally imposing a peace settlement in 493 BC on Ionia that was generally considered to be both just and fair. The <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a> constituted the first major conflict between Greece and the Achaemenid Empire, and as such represents the first phase of the Greco-Persian Wars. Asia Minor had been brought back into the Persian fold, but Darius had vowed to punish Athens and Eretria for their support of the revolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup> Moreover, seeing that the political situation in Greece posed a continued threat to the stability of his Empire, he decided to embark on the conquest of all of Greece. The first campaign of the invasion was to bring the territories in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balkan" title="Balkan">Balkan</a> peninsula back within the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_companion_to_Ancient_Macedonia_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_companion_to_Ancient_Macedonia-105">[99]</a></sup> The Persian grip over these territories had loosened following the Ionian Revolt. In 492 BC, the Persian general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mardonius_(general)" title="Mardonius (general)">Mardonius</a> re-subjugated <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> and made <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedon</a> a fully <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subordinate" title="Subordinate">subordinate</a> part of the empire; it had been a vassal as early as the late 6th century BC but retained a great deal of autonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_companion_to_Ancient_Macedonia_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-A_companion_to_Ancient_Macedonia-105">[99]</a></sup> However, in 490 BC the Persian forces were defeated by the Athenians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">Battle of Marathon</a> and Darius would die before having the chance to launch an invasion of Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_king_fighting_hoplites,_Cimmerian_Bosphorus_intaglio_(composite).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="644" data-file-width="1366" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Achaemenid_king_fighting_hoplites%2C_Cimmerian_Bosphorus_intaglio_%28composite%29.jpg/330px-Achaemenid_king_fighting_hoplites%2C_Cimmerian_Bosphorus_intaglio_%28composite%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Achaemenid_king_fighting_hoplites%2C_Cimmerian_Bosphorus_intaglio_%28composite%29.jpg/495px-Achaemenid_king_fighting_hoplites%2C_Cimmerian_Bosphorus_intaglio_%28composite%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Achaemenid_king_fighting_hoplites%2C_Cimmerian_Bosphorus_intaglio_%28composite%29.jpg/660px-Achaemenid_king_fighting_hoplites%2C_Cimmerian_Bosphorus_intaglio_%28composite%29.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_king_fighting_hoplites,_Cimmerian_Bosphorus_intaglio_(composite).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Achaemenid king fighting hoplites, seal and seal holder, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cimmerian_Bosporus" title="Cimmerian Bosporus">Cimmerian Bosporus</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> (485–465 BC, Old Persian <i>Xšayārša</i> "Hero Among Kings"), son of <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius I</a>, vowed to complete the job. He organized a massive invasion aiming to conquer <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>. His army entered Greece from the north in the spring of 480 BC, meeting little or no resistance through <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Greece)" title="Macedonia (Greece)">Macedonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a>, but was delayed by a small Greek force for three days at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">Thermopylae</a>. A simultaneous naval battle at Artemisium was tactically indecisive as large storms destroyed ships from both sides. The battle was stopped prematurely when the Greeks received news of the defeat at Thermopylae and retreated. The battle was a strategic victory for the Persians, giving them uncontested control of Artemisium and the Aegean Sea.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Following his victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">Battle of Thermopylae</a>, Xerxes sacked the evacuated city of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and prepared to meet the Greeks at the strategic <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Corinth" title="Isthmus of Corinth">Isthmus of Corinth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Saronic_Gulf" title="Saronic Gulf">Saronic Gulf</a>. In 480 BC the Greeks won a decisive victory over the Persian fleet at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a> and forced Xerxes to retire to <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-107">[101]</a></sup> The land army which he left in Greece under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mardonius_(general)" title="Mardonius (general)">Mardonius</a> retook Athens but was eventually destroyed in 479 BC at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">Battle of Plataea</a>. The final defeat of the Persians at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mycale" title="Battle of Mycale">Mycale</a> encouraged the Greek cities of Asia to revolt, and the Persians lost all of their territories in Europe; Macedonia once again became independent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_92-5"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-92">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_phase">Cultural phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cultural phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> was assassinated, he was succeeded by his eldest surviving son <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_I" title="Artaxerxes I">Artaxerxes I</a>. It was during his reign that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamite" title="Elamite">Elamite</a> ceased to be the language of government,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and Aramaic gained in importance. It was probably during this reign that the solar calendar was introduced as the national calendar. Under Artaxerxes I, <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> became the <i>de facto</i> religion of state.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>After Persia had been defeated at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Eurymedon" title="Battle of Eurymedon">Battle of Eurymedon</a> (469 or 466 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup>), military action between Greece and Persia was halted. When Artaxerxes I took power, he introduced a new Persian strategy of weakening the Athenians by funding their enemies in Greece. This indirectly caused the Athenians to move the treasury of the <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">Delian League</a> from the island of <a href="/wiki/Delos" title="Delos">Delos</a> to the Athenian acropolis. This funding practice inevitably prompted renewed fighting in 450 BC, where the Greeks attacked at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis_in_Cyprus_(450_BC)" title="Battle of Salamis in Cyprus (450 BC)">Battle of Cyprus</a>. After <a href="/wiki/Cimon" title="Cimon">Cimon</a>'s failure to attain much in this expedition, the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Callias" title="Peace of Callias">Peace of Callias</a> was agreed between <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argos,_Peloponnese" title="Argos, Peloponnese">Argos</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> in 449 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Artaxerxes I offered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_asylum" title="Political asylum">asylum</a> to <a href="/wiki/Themistocles" title="Themistocles">Themistocles</a>, who was the winner of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a>, after Themistocles was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ostracized" title="Ostracized">ostracized</a> from <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>. Also, Artaxerxes I gave him <a href="/wiki/Magnesia_on_the_Maeander" title="Magnesia on the Maeander">Magnesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myus" title="Myus">Myus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lampsacus" title="Lampsacus">Lampsacus</a> to maintain him in bread, meat, and wine. In addition, Artaxerxes I gave him <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaescepsis" title="Palaescepsis">Palaescepsis</a> to provide him with clothes, and he also gave him <a href="/wiki/Percote" title="Percote">Percote</a> with bedding for his house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_gold_ornaments,70.142.6-.11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Achaemenid_gold_ornaments%2C70.142.6-.11.jpg/220px-Achaemenid_gold_ornaments%2C70.142.6-.11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Achaemenid_gold_ornaments%2C70.142.6-.11.jpg/330px-Achaemenid_gold_ornaments%2C70.142.6-.11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Achaemenid_gold_ornaments%2C70.142.6-.11.jpg/440px-Achaemenid_gold_ornaments%2C70.142.6-.11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_gold_ornaments,70.142.6-.11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Achaemenid gold ornaments, <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>When Artaxerxes died in 424 BC at <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>, his body was taken to the tomb already built for him in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naqsh-e_Rustam" title="Naqsh-e Rustam">Naqsh-e Rustam</a> Necropolis. It was Persian tradition that kings begin constructing their own tombs while they were still alive. Artaxerxes I was immediately succeeded by his eldest and only legitimate son, <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_II" title="Xerxes II">Xerxes II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuhrt2013880_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuhrt2013880-112">[106]</a></sup> However, after a few days on the throne, he was assassinated while drunk by Pharnacyas and Menostanes on the orders of his illegitimate brother <a href="/wiki/Sogdianus" title="Sogdianus">Sogdianus</a>, who apparently had gained the support of his regions. He reigned for six months and fifteen days before being captured by his half-brother, <a href="/wiki/Darius_II" title="Darius II">Ochus</a>, who had rebelled against him. Sogdianus was executed by being <a href="/wiki/Suffocation_in_ash" title="Suffocation in ash">suffocated in ash</a> because Ochus had promised he would not die by the sword, by poison or by hunger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup> Ochus then took the royal name Darius II. Darius' ability to defend his position on the throne ended the short power vacuum.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>From 412 BC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_II_of_Persia" title="Darius II of Persia">Darius II</a>, at the insistence of <a href="/wiki/Tissaphernes" title="Tissaphernes">Tissaphernes</a>, gave support first to Athens, then to Sparta, but in 407 BC, Darius' son <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Younger" title="Cyrus the Younger">Cyrus the Younger</a> was appointed to replace Tissaphernes and aid was given entirely to Sparta which finally defeated Athens in 404 BC. In the same year, Darius fell ill and died in Babylon. His death gave an Egyptian rebel named <a href="/wiki/Amyrtaeus" title="Amyrtaeus">Amyrtaeus</a> the opportunity to throw off <a href="/wiki/Twenty-seventh_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-seventh Dynasty of Egypt">Persian control over Egypt</a>. At his death bed, Darius' Babylonian wife <a href="/wiki/Parysatis" title="Parysatis">Parysatis</a> pleaded with him to have her second eldest son Cyrus (the Younger) crowned, but Darius refused. Queen Parysatis favoured Cyrus more than her eldest son <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a>. <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> relates (probably on the authority of <a href="/wiki/Ctesias" title="Ctesias">Ctesias</a>) that the displaced Tissaphernes came to the new king on his coronation day to warn him that his younger brother Cyrus (the Younger) was preparing to assassinate him during the ceremony. Artaxerxes had Cyrus arrested and would have had him executed if their mother Parysatis had not intervened. Cyrus was then sent back as Satrap of Lydia, where he prepared an armed rebellion. Cyrus assembled a large army, including a contingent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ten_Thousand_Greek_mercenaries" title="Ten Thousand Greek mercenaries">Ten Thousand Greek mercenaries</a>, and made his way deeper into Persia. The army of Cyrus was stopped by the royal Persian army of <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cunaxa" title="Battle of Cunaxa">Cunaxa</a> in 401 BC, where Cyrus was killed. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ten_Thousand_Greek_Mercenaries" title="Ten Thousand Greek Mercenaries">Ten Thousand Greek Mercenaries</a> including <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> were now deep in Persian territory and were at risk of attack. So they searched for others to offer their services to but eventually had to return to Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-107">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a> was the longest reigning of the Achaemenid kings and it was during this 45-year period of relative peace and stability that many of the monuments of the era were constructed. Artaxerxes moved the capital back to <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, which he greatly extended. Also, the summer capital at <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a> was lavishly extended with gilded columns and roof tiles of silver and copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup> The extraordinary innovation of the Zoroastrian shrines can also be dated to his reign, and it was probably during this period that Zoroastrianism spread from <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> throughout <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. The construction of temples, though serving a religious purpose, was not a purely selfless act, as they also served as an important source of income. From the Babylonian kings, the Achaemenids had taken over the concept of a mandatory temple tax, a one-tenth tithe which all inhabitants paid to the temple nearest to their land or another source of income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup> A share of this income called the <i>Quppu Sha Sharri</i>, "king's chest"—an ingenious institution originally introduced by <a href="/wiki/Nabonidus" title="Nabonidus">Nabonidus</a>—was then turned over to the ruler. In retrospect, Artaxerxes is generally regarded as an amiable man who lacked the moral fiber to be a really successful ruler. However, six centuries later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ardeshir_I" title="Ardeshir I">Ardeshir I</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>, would consider himself Artaxerxes' successor, a grand testimony to the importance of Artaxerxes to the Persian psyche.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1035" data-file-width="1359" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Achaemenid_Empire.gif/330px-Achaemenid_Empire.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Achaemenid_Empire.gif/495px-Achaemenid_Empire.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Achaemenid_Empire.gif/660px-Achaemenid_Empire.gif 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Persian Empire timeline including important events and territorial evolution – 550–323 BC</div></div></div>
<p>Artaxerxes II became involved in a war with Persia's erstwhile allies, the <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Spartans</a>, who, under <a href="/wiki/Agesilaus_II" title="Agesilaus II">Agesilaus II</a>, invaded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. In order to redirect the Spartans' attention to Greek affairs, Artaxerxes II subsidized their enemies: in particular the <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athenians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinthians</a>. These subsidies helped to engage the <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Spartans</a> in what would become known as the <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_War" title="Corinthian War">Corinthian War</a>. In 387 BC, Artaxerxes II betrayed his allies and came to an arrangement with Sparta, and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Antalcidas" title="Treaty of Antalcidas">Treaty of Antalcidas</a> he forced his erstwhile allies to come to terms. This treaty restored control of the Greek cities of <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aeolis" title="Aeolis">Aeolis</a> on the Anatolian coast to the Persians while giving Sparta dominance on the Greek mainland. In 385 BC he <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes%27_II_Cadusian_Campaign" title="Artaxerxes' II Cadusian Campaign">campaigned against the Cadusians</a>. Although successful against the Greeks, Artaxerxes II had more trouble with the <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egyptians</a>, who had successfully revolted against him at the beginning of his reign. An attempt to reconquer Egypt in 373 BC was completely unsuccessful, but in his waning years the Persians did manage to defeat a joint Egyptian–Spartan effort to conquer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Phoenicia" title="Achaemenid Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>. He quashed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolt_of_the_Satraps" title="Revolt of the Satraps">Revolt of the Satraps</a> in 372–362 BC. He is reported to have had a number of wives. His main wife was <a href="/wiki/Stateira_(wife_of_Artaxerxes_II)" title="Stateira (wife of Artaxerxes II)">Stateira</a>, until she was poisoned by Artaxerxes II's mother Parysatis in about 400 BC. Another chief wife was a Greek woman of <a href="/wiki/Phocaea" title="Phocaea">Phocaea</a> named Aspasia (not the same as the concubine of <a href="/wiki/Pericles" title="Pericles">Pericles</a>). Artaxerxes II is said to have had more than 115 sons from 350 wives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 358 BC Artaxerxes II died and was succeeded by his son <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a>. In 355 BC, Artaxerxes III forced <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> to conclude a peace which required the city's forces to leave <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and to acknowledge the independence of its rebellious allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[112]</a></sup> Artaxerxes started a campaign against the rebellious <a href="/wiki/Cadusii" title="Cadusii">Cadusians</a>, but he managed to appease both of the Cadusian kings. One individual who successfully emerged from this campaign was Darius Codomannus, who later occupied the Persian throne as <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Artaxerxes III then ordered the disbanding of all the satrapal armies of Asia Minor, as he felt that they could no longer guarantee peace in the west and was concerned that these armies equipped the western satraps with the means to revolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PersianArmy_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-PersianArmy-119">[113]</a></sup> The order was however ignored by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artabazos_II_of_Phrygia" title="Artabazos II of Phrygia">Artabazos II of Phrygia</a>, who asked for the help of Athens in a rebellion against the king. Athens sent assistance to <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Orontid_dynasty#Orontid_Kings_and_satraps_of_Armenia" title="Orontid dynasty">Orontes of Mysia</a> also supported Artabazos and the combined forces managed to defeat the forces sent by Artaxerxes III in 354 BC. However, in 353 BC, they were defeated by Artaxerxes III's army and were disbanded. Orontes was pardoned by the king, while Artabazos fled to the safety of the court of <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a>. In around 351 BC, Artaxerxes embarked on a campaign to recover Egypt, which had revolted under his father, Artaxerxes II. At the same time, a rebellion had broken out in Asia Minor, which, being supported by <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>, threatened to become serious. Levying a vast army, Artaxerxes marched into Egypt and engaged <a href="/wiki/Nectanebo_II" title="Nectanebo II">Nectanebo II</a>. After a year of fighting the Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaoh</a>, Nectanebo inflicted a crushing defeat on the Persians with the support of mercenaries led by the Greek generals Diophantus and Lamius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup> Artaxerxes was compelled to retreat and postpone his plans to reconquer Egypt. Soon after this defeat, there were rebellions in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Phoenicia" title="Achaemenid Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Anatolia#Achaemenid_Empire" title="History of Anatolia">Asia Minor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history_of_Cyprus#Persian_period" title="Ancient history of Cyprus">Cyprus</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti
