srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Darius_detail_on_the_Darius_vase.jpg/285px-Darius_detail_on_the_Darius_vase.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Darius_detail_on_the_Darius_vase.jpg/380px-Darius_detail_on_the_Darius_vase.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Detail of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius</a>, with a label in Greek (ΔΑΡΕΙΟΣ, top right) giving his name.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In 343 BC, Artaxerxes committed responsibility for the suppression of the Cyprian rebels to <a href="/wiki/Idrieus" title="Idrieus">Idrieus</a>, prince of <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a>, who employed 8,000 Greek mercenaries and forty <a href="/wiki/Trireme" title="Trireme">triremes</a>, commanded by <a href="/wiki/Phocion" title="Phocion">Phocion</a> the Athenian, and Evagoras, son of the elder <a href="/wiki/Evagoras_I" title="Evagoras I">Evagoras</a>, the Cypriot monarch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-122">[116]</a></sup> Idrieus succeeded in reducing Cyprus. Artaxerxes initiated a counter-offensive against <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a> by commanding <a href="/wiki/Belesys" title="Belesys">Belesys</a>, satrap of Syria, and <a href="/wiki/Mazaeus" title="Mazaeus">Mazaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cilicia_(satrapy)" title="Cilicia (satrapy)">satrap of Cilicia</a>, to invade the city and to keep the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> in check. Both satraps suffered crushing defeats at the hands of Tennes, the Sidonese king, who was aided by 40,000 Greek mercenaries sent to him by <a href="/wiki/Nectanebo_II" title="Nectanebo II">Nectanebo II</a> and commanded by <a href="/wiki/Mentor_of_Rhodes" title="Mentor of Rhodes">Mentor of Rhodes</a>. As a result, the Persian forces were driven out of <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-122">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>After this, Artaxerxes personally led an army of 330,000 men against <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a>. Artaxerxes' army comprised 300,000-foot soldiers, 30,000 <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a>, 300 triremes, and 500 transports or provision ships. After gathering this army, he sought assistance from the Greeks. Though refused aid by <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, he succeeded in obtaining a thousand Theban heavy-armed hoplites under Lacrates, three thousand Argives under Nicostratus, and six thousand Æolians, <a href="/wiki/Ionians" title="Ionians">Ionians</a>, and Dorians from the Greek cities of Asia Minor. This Greek support was numerically small, amounting to no more than 10,000 men, but it formed, together with the Greek mercenaries from Egypt who went over to him afterward, the force on which he placed his chief reliance, and to which the ultimate success of his expedition was mainly due. The approach of Artaxerxes sufficiently weakened the resolution of Tennes that he endeavoured to purchase his own pardon by delivering up 100 principal citizens of Sidon into the hands of the Persian king and then admitting Artaxerxes within the defences of the town. Artaxerxes had the 100 citizens transfixed with javelins, and when 500 more came out as supplicants to seek his mercy, Artaxerxes consigned them to the same fate. Sidon was then burnt to the ground, either by Artaxerxes or by the Sidonian citizens. Forty thousand people died in the conflagration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-122">[116]</a></sup> Artaxerxes sold the ruins at a high price to speculators, who calculated on reimbursing themselves by the treasures which they hoped to dig out from among the ashes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[117]</a></sup> Tennes was later put to death by Artaxerxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovetoknow_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lovetoknow-124">[118]</a></sup> Artaxerxes later sent Jews who supported the revolt to <a href="/wiki/Hyrcania" title="Hyrcania">Hyrcania</a> on the south coast of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_conquest_of_Egypt">Second conquest of Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Second conquest of Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HibisGate3Dareios1AmunRaKamutef.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4800" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/HibisGate3Dareios1AmunRaKamutef.jpg/170px-HibisGate3Dareios1AmunRaKamutef.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/HibisGate3Dareios1AmunRaKamutef.jpg/255px-HibisGate3Dareios1AmunRaKamutef.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/HibisGate3Dareios1AmunRaKamutef.jpg/340px-HibisGate3Dareios1AmunRaKamutef.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HibisGate3Dareios1AmunRaKamutef.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relief showing Darius I offering lettuces to the Egyptian deity <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amun-Ra_Kamutef" title="Amun-Ra Kamutef">Amun-Ra Kamutef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Hibis" title="Temple of Hibis">Temple of Hibis</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Darius_I_statue_list_of_subject_countries.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2300" data-file-width="1459" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Darius_I_statue_list_of_subject_countries.jpg/170px-Darius_I_statue_list_of_subject_countries.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Darius_I_statue_list_of_subject_countries.jpg/255px-Darius_I_statue_list_of_subject_countries.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Darius_I_statue_list_of_subject_countries.jpg/340px-Darius_I_statue_list_of_subject_countries.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Darius_I_statue_list_of_subject_countries.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 24 countries subject to the Achaemenid Empire at the time of Darius, on the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_statue_of_Darius_I" title="Egyptian statue of Darius I">Egyptian statue of Darius I</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The reduction of Sidon was followed closely by the invasion of Egypt. In 343 BC, Artaxerxes, in addition to his 330,000 Persians, had now a force of 14,000 Greeks furnished by the Greek cities of Asia Minor: 4,000 under <a href="/wiki/Mentor_of_Rhodes" title="Mentor of Rhodes">Mentor</a>, consisting of the troops that he had brought to the aid of Tennes from Egypt; 3,000 sent by Argos; and 1,000 from Thebes. He divided these troops into three bodies, and placed at the head of each a Persian and a Greek. The Greek commanders were Lacrates of Thebes, <a href="/wiki/Mentor_of_Rhodes" title="Mentor of Rhodes">Mentor of Rhodes</a> and Nicostratus of Argos while the Persians were led by Rhossaces, Aristazanes, and <a href="/wiki/Bagoas" title="Bagoas">Bagoas</a>, the chief of the eunuchs. <a href="/wiki/Nectanebo_II" title="Nectanebo II">Nectanebo II</a> resisted with an army of 100,000 of whom 20,000 were Greek mercenaries. Nectanebo II occupied the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> and its various branches with his large navy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The character of the country, intersected by numerous canals and full of strongly fortified towns, was in his favour and Nectanebo II might have been expected to offer a prolonged, if not even a successful, resistance. However, he lacked good generals, and, over-confident in his own powers of command, he was out-maneuvered by the Greek mercenary generals, and his forces were eventually defeated by the combined Persian armies. After his defeat, Nectanebo hastily fled to <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a>, leaving the fortified towns to be defended by their garrisons. These garrisons consisted of partly <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> and partly Egyptian troops; between whom jealousies and suspicions were easily sown by the Persian leaders. As a result, the Persians were able to rapidly reduce numerous towns across Lower Egypt and were advancing upon Memphis when Nectanebo decided to quit the country and flee southwards to <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_122-3"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-122">[116]</a></sup> The Persian army completely routed the Egyptians and occupied the Lower Delta of the Nile. Following Nectanebo fleeing to Ethiopia, all of Egypt submitted to Artaxerxes. The Jews in Egypt were sent either to <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> or to the south coast of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>, the same location that the Jews of <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a> had earlier been sent.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>After this victory over the Egyptians, Artaxerxes had the city walls destroyed, started a reign of terror, and set about looting all the temples. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> gained a significant amount of wealth from this looting. Artaxerxes also raised high taxes and attempted to weaken <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> enough that it could never revolt against Persia. For the 10 years that Persia controlled Egypt, believers in the native religion were persecuted and sacred books were stolen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Egyptloot_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Egyptloot-127">[121]</a></sup> Before he returned to Persia, he appointed Pherendares as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Achaemenid_Egypt" title="History of Achaemenid Egypt">satrap of Egypt</a>. With the wealth gained from his reconquering Egypt, Artaxerxes was able to amply reward his mercenaries. He then returned to his capital having successfully completed his invasion of Egypt.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>After his success in Egypt, Artaxerxes returned to Persia and spent the next few years effectively quelling insurrections in various parts of the Empire so that a few years after his conquest of Egypt, the Persian Empire was firmly under his control. Egypt remained a part of the Persian Empire until <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>'s conquest of Egypt.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>After the conquest of Egypt, there were no more revolts or rebellions against Artaxerxes. Mentor and <a href="/wiki/Bagoas" title="Bagoas">Bagoas</a>, the two generals who had most distinguished themselves in the Egyptian campaign, were advanced to posts of the highest importance. Mentor, who was governor of the entire Asiatic seaboard, was successful in reducing to subjection many of the chiefs who during the recent troubles had rebelled against Persian rule. In the course of a few years, Mentor and his forces were able to bring the whole Asian Mediterranean coast into complete submission and dependence.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Bagoas went back to the Persian capital with Artaxerxes, where he took a leading role in the internal administration of the Empire and maintained tranquillity throughout the rest of the Empire. During the last six years of the reign of Artaxerxes III, the Persian Empire was governed by a vigorous and successful government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_122-4"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-122">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Persian forces in <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Lycia</a> regained control of the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and took over much of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>' former island empire. In response, <a href="/wiki/Isocrates" title="Isocrates">Isocrates</a> of Athens started giving speeches calling for a 'crusade against the barbarians' but there was not enough strength left in any of the Greek city-states to answer his call.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conspiracy_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-conspiracy-128">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although there were no rebellions in the Persian Empire itself, the growing power and territory of <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a> (against which <a href="/wiki/Demosthenes" title="Demosthenes">Demosthenes</a> was in vain warning the Athenians) attracted the attention of Artaxerxes. In response, he ordered that Persian influence was to be used to check and constrain the rising power and influence of the Macedonian kingdom. In 340 BC, a Persian force was dispatched to assist the <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thracian prince</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cersobleptes" title="Cersobleptes">Cersobleptes</a>, to maintain his independence. Sufficient effective aid was given to the city of <a href="/wiki/Perinthus" title="Perinthus">Perinthus</a> that the numerous and well-appointed army with which Philip had commenced his siege of the city was compelled to give up the attempt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_122-5"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-122">[116]</a></sup> By the last year of Artaxerxes' rule, Philip II already had plans in place for an invasion of the Persian Empire, which would crown his career, but the Greeks would not unite with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 338 BC Artaxerxes was poisoned by <a href="/wiki/Bagoas" title="Bagoas">Bagoas</a> with the assistance of a physician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant2002769_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant2002769-130">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall_of_the_empire">Fall of the empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fall of the empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_2013-05-16_16-25-06_BW.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2133" data-file-width="3562" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_2013-05-16_16-25-06_BW.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_2013-05-16_16-25-06_BW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_2013-05-16_16-25-06_BW.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_2013-05-16_16-25-06_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_2013-05-16_16-25-06_BW.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_2013-05-16_16-25-06_BW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_2013-05-16_16-25-06_BW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Battle of Issus</a>, between <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> on horseback to the left, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_III_of_Persia" title="Darius III of Persia">Darius III</a> in the chariot to the right, represented in a Pompeii <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> dated 1st century BC – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naples_National_Archaeological_Museum" title="Naples National Archaeological Museum">Naples National Archaeological Museum</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius,_the_Persian_king.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="539" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg/220px-Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg/330px-Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg/440px-Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius,_the_Persian_king.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander's first victory over Darius, the Persian king depicted in medieval European style in the 15th century romance <i>The History of Alexander's Battles</i></div></div></div>
<p>Artaxerxes III was succeeded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_IV_Arses" title="Artaxerxes IV Arses">Artaxerxes IV Arses</a>, who before he could act was also poisoned by Bagoas. Bagoas is further said to have killed not only all Arses' children, but many of the other princes of the land. Bagoas then placed <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a>, a nephew of Artaxerxes IV, on the throne. Darius III, previously <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orontid_Dynasty" title="Orontid Dynasty">Satrap of Armenia</a>, personally forced Bagoas to swallow poison. In 334 BC, when Darius was just succeeding in subduing Egypt again, Alexander and his battle-hardened troops <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">invaded Asia Minor</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> (Alexander III of Macedon) defeated the Persian armies at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Granicus" title="Battle of Granicus">Granicus</a> (334 BC), followed by <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Issus</a> (333 BC), and lastly at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Gaugamela</a> (331 BC). Afterwards, he marched on <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> which surrendered in early 330 BC. From Persepolis, Alexander headed north to Pasargadae, where he visited the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Cyrus" title="Tomb of Cyrus">tomb of Cyrus</a>, the burial of the man whom he had heard of from the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyropedia" title="Cyropedia">Cyropedia</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the ensuing chaos created by Alexander's invasion of Persia, Cyrus's tomb was broken into and most of its luxuries were looted. When Alexander reached the tomb, he was horrified by the manner in which it had been treated, and questioned the Magi, putting them on trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleveland_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cleveland-131">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[126]</a></sup> By some accounts, Alexander's decision to put the Magi on trial was more an attempt to undermine their influence and display his own power than a show of concern for Cyrus's tomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[127]</a></sup> Regardless, Alexander the Great ordered Aristobulus to improve the tomb's condition and restore its interior, showing respect for Cyrus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleveland_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cleveland-131">[125]</a></sup> From there he headed to <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a>, where Darius III had sought refuge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crystalinks.com_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-crystalinks.com-134">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>Darius III was taken prisoner by <a href="/wiki/Bessus" title="Bessus">Bessus</a>, his <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> and kinsman. As Alexander approached, Bessus had his men murder Darius III and then declared himself Darius' successor, as Artaxerxes V, before retreating into Central Asia leaving Darius' body in the road to delay Alexander, who brought it to Persepolis for an honourable funeral. Bessus would then create a coalition of his forces, in order to create an army to defend against Alexander. Before Bessus could fully unite with his confederates at the eastern part of the empire,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[129]</a></sup> Alexander, fearing the danger of Bessus gaining control, found him, put him on trial in a Persian court under his control, and ordered his execution in a "cruel and barbarous manner."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander generally kept the original Achaemenid administrative structure, leading some scholars to dub him as "the last of the Achaemenids".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[131]</a></sup> Upon Alexander's death in 323 BC, his empire was divided among his generals, the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a>, resulting in a number of smaller states. The largest of these, which held sway over the Iranian plateau, was the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, ruled by Alexander's general <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a>. Native Iranian rule would be restored by the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> of northeastern Iran over the course of the 2nd century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descendants_in_later_Persian_dynasties">Descendants in later Persian dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Descendants in later Persian dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dt>"Frataraka" Governors of the Seleucid Empire</dt></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KINGS_of_PERSIS._V%C4%81dfrad%C4%81d_(Autophradates)_I._3rd_century_BC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="382" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/KINGS_of_PERSIS._V%C4%81dfrad%C4%81d_%28Autophradates%29_I._3rd_century_BC.jpg/220px-KINGS_of_PERSIS._V%C4%81dfrad%C4%81d_%28Autophradates%29_I._3rd_century_BC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/KINGS_of_PERSIS._V%C4%81dfrad%C4%81d_%28Autophradates%29_I._3rd_century_BC.jpg/330px-KINGS_of_PERSIS._V%C4%81dfrad%C4%81d_%28Autophradates%29_I._3rd_century_BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/KINGS_of_PERSIS._V%C4%81dfrad%C4%81d_%28Autophradates%29_I._3rd_century_BC.jpg/440px-KINGS_of_PERSIS._V%C4%81dfrad%C4%81d_%28Autophradates%29_I._3rd_century_BC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KINGS_of_PERSIS._V%C4%81dfrad%C4%81d_(Autophradates)_I._3rd_century_BC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Frataraka" title="Frataraka">Frataraka</a> dynasty ruler <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vadfradad_I" title="Vadfradad I">Vadfradad I</a> (Autophradates I). 3rd century BC. Istakhr (Persepolis) mint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[133]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Frataraka" title="Frataraka">Frataraka</a></div>
<p>Several later Persian rulers, forming the <i><a href="/wiki/Frataraka" title="Frataraka">Frataraka</a></i> dynasty, are known to have acted as representatives of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucids" title="Seleucids">Seleucids</a> in the region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F%C4%81rs" title="Fārs">Fārs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EI_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-EI-140">[134]</a></sup> They ruled from the end of the 3rd century BC to the beginning of the 2nd century BC, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vahbarz" title="Vahbarz">Vahbarz</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/V%C4%81dfrad%C4%81d_I" title="Vādfradād I">Vādfradād I</a> obtained independence circa 150 BC, when Seleucid power waned in the areas of southwestern Persia and the Persian Gulf region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EI_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-EI-140">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Kings of Persis, under the Parthian Empire</dt></dl>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Persis" title="Kings of Persis">Kings of Persis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KINGS_of_PERSIS._D%C4%81r%C4%93v_(Darios)_I._2nd_century_BC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/KINGS_of_PERSIS._D%C4%81r%C4%93v_%28Darios%29_I._2nd_century_BC.jpg/220px-KINGS_of_PERSIS._D%C4%81r%C4%93v_%28Darios%29_I._2nd_century_BC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/KINGS_of_PERSIS._D%C4%81r%C4%93v_%28Darios%29_I._2nd_century_BC.jpg/330px-KINGS_of_PERSIS._D%C4%81r%C4%93v_%28Darios%29_I._2nd_century_BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/KINGS_of_PERSIS._D%C4%81r%C4%93v_%28Darios%29_I._2nd_century_BC.jpg/440px-KINGS_of_PERSIS._D%C4%81r%C4%93v_%28Darios%29_I._2nd_century_BC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KINGS_of_PERSIS._D%C4%81r%C4%93v_(Darios)_I._2nd_century_BC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D%C4%81r%C4%93v_I" title="Dārēv I">Dārēv I</a> (Darios I) used for the first time the title of <i>mlk</i> (King). 2nd century BC.</div></div></div>
<p>During an apparent transitional period, corresponding to the reigns of Vādfradād II and another uncertain king, no titles of authority appeared on the reverse of their coins. The earlier title <i>prtrk' zy alhaya</i> (Frataraka) had disappeared. Under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D%C4%81r%C4%93v_I" title="Dārēv I">Dārēv I</a> however, the new title of <i>mlk</i>, or king, appeared, sometimes with the mention of <i>prs</i> (Persis), suggesting that the kings of Persis had become independent rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNG_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNG-141">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arsacid" title="Arsacid">Arsacid</a> king <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_I_of_Parthia" title="Mithridates I of Parthia">Mithridates I</a> (c. 171–138 BC) took control of <a href="/wiki/Persis" title="Persis">Persis</a>, he left the Persian dynasts in office, known as the <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Persis" title="Kings of Persis">Kings of Persis</a>, and they were allowed to continue minting coins with the title of <i>mlk</i> ("King").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EI_140-2"><a href="#cite_note-EI-140">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Sasanian Empire</dt></dl>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a></div>
<p>With the reign of Šābuhr, the son of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81pag" title="Pāpag">Pāpag</a>, the kingdom of Persis then became a part of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>. Šābuhr's brother and successor, Ardaxšir (Artaxerxes) V, defeated the last legitimate Parthian king, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artabanus_V_of_Parthia" title="Artabanus V of Parthia">Artabanos V</a> in 224 AD, and was crowned at <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ardax%C5%A1ir_I" title="Ardaxšir I">Ardaxšir I</a> (Ardashir I), <i>šāhanšāh ī Ērān</i>, becoming the first king of the new <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNG_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-CNG-141">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Kingdom of Pontus</dt></dl>
<p>The Achaemenid line would also be carried on through the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Pontus" title="Kingdom of Pontus">Kingdom of Pontus</a>, based in the <a href="/wiki/Pontus_(region)" title="Pontus (region)">Pontus</a> region of northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. This Pontic Kingdom, a state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> origin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranicaonline.org_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-iranicaonline.org-145">[139]</a></sup> may even have been directly related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius the Great</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_dynasty" title="Achaemenid dynasty">Achaemenid dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranicaonline.org_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-iranicaonline.org-145">[139]</a></sup> It was founded by <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_I_of_Pontus" title="Mithridates I of Pontus">Mithridates I</a> in 281 BC and lasted until its conquest by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> in 63 BC. The kingdom grew to its largest extent under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mithridates_VI" title="Mithridates VI">Mithridates VI</a> the Great, who conquered <a href="/wiki/Colchis" title="Colchis">Colchis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a>, the Greek colonies of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chersonesus_Taurica" title="Chersonesus Taurica">Tauric Chersonesos</a> and for a brief time the Roman province of <a href="/wiki/Asia_(Roman_province)" title="Asia (Roman province)">Asia</a>. Thus, this Persian dynasty managed to survive and prosper in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_world" title="Hellenistic world">Hellenistic world</a> while the main Persian Empire had fallen.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Despite Greek influence on the Kingdom of Pontus, Pontics continued to maintain their Achaemenid lineage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranicaonline.org_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-iranicaonline.org-145">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sphinx_Darius_Louvre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="877" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Sphinx_Darius_Louvre.jpg/170px-Sphinx_Darius_Louvre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Sphinx_Darius_Louvre.jpg/255px-Sphinx_Darius_Louvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Sphinx_Darius_Louvre.jpg/340px-Sphinx_Darius_Louvre.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sphinx_Darius_Louvre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Winged <a href="/wiki/Sphinx" title="Sphinx">sphinx</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Darius_in_Susa" title="Palace of Darius in Susa">Palace of Darius in Susa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a></div></div></div>
<p>Both the later dynasties of the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanians</a> would on occasion claim Achaemenid descent. Recently there has been some corroboration for the Parthian claim to Achaemenid ancestry via the possibility of an inherited disease (<a href="/wiki/Neurofibromatosis" title="Neurofibromatosis">neurofibromatosis</a>) demonstrated by the physical descriptions of rulers and from the evidence of familial disease on ancient coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_of_decline">Causes of decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Causes of decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Part of the cause of the Empire's decline had been the heavy tax burden put upon the state, which eventually led to economic decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[142]</a></sup> An estimate of the tribute imposed on the subject nations was up to U.S. $180M per year. This does not include the material goods and supplies that were supplied as taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_p. 363_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_p._363-149">[143]</a></sup> After the high overhead of government—the military, the bureaucracy, whatever the satraps could safely dip into the coffers for themselves—this money went into the royal treasury. According to Diodorus, at Persepolis, Alexander III found some 180,000 <a href="/wiki/Attic_talent" title="Attic talent">Attic talents</a> of silver besides the additional treasure the Macedonians were carrying that already had been seized in Damascus by <a href="/wiki/Parmenion" title="Parmenion">Parmenion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[144]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title='citation is a rarely accepted "fact" that is based on a most unreliable account (Diodorus) who moreover himself lived two centuries later (October 2015)'>better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This amounted to U.S. $2.7B. On top of this, Darius III had taken 8,000 talents with him on his flight to the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_p. 363_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_p._363-149">[143]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title='citation is a rarely accepted "fact" that is based on a most unreliable account (Diodorus) who moreover himself lived two centuries later (October 2015)'>better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Alexander put this static hoard back into the economy, and upon his death, some 130,000 talents had been spent on the building of cities, dockyards, temples, and the payment of the troops, besides the ordinary government expenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[145]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title='citation is a rarely accepted "fact" that is based on a most unreliable account (Diodorus) who moreover himself lived two centuries later (October 2015)'>better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Additionally, one of the satraps, Harpalus, had made off to Greece with some 6,000 talents, which Athens used to rebuild its economy after seizing it during the struggles with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corinthian_League" title="Corinthian League">Corinthian League</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[146]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title='citation is a rarely accepted "fact" that is based on a most unreliable account (Diodorus) who moreover himself lived two centuries later (October 2015)'>better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Due to the flood of money from Alexander's hoard entering Greece, however, a disruption in the economy occurred, in agriculture, banking, rents, the great increase in mercenary soldiers that cash allowed the wealthy, and an increase in piracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[147]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title='citation is a rarely accepted "fact" that is based on a most unreliable account (Diodorus) who moreover himself lived two centuries later (October 2015)'>better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Another factor contributing to the decline of the Empire, in the period following Xerxes, was its failure to ever mold the many subject nations into a whole; the creation of a national identity was never attempted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[148]</a></sup> This lack of cohesion eventually affected the efficiency of the military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg/220px-Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg/330px-Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg/440px-Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daric" title="Daric">Daric</a> of Artaxerxes II</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> founded the empire as a multi-<a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> empire, governed from four capital cities: <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a>. The Achaemenids allowed a certain amount of regional autonomy in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrapy</a> system. A satrapy was an administrative unit, usually organized on a geographical basis. A '<a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a>' (governor) was the governor who administered the region, a 'general' supervised military recruitment and ensured order, and a 'state secretary' kept the official records. The general and the state secretary reported directly to the satrap as well as the central government. At differing times, there were between 20 and 30 satrapies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cyrus the Great created an organized army including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Immortals" title="Persian Immortals">Immortals</a> unit, consisting of 10,000 highly trained soldiers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant2002261_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant2002261-157">[151]</a></sup> Cyrus also formed an innovative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postal_system" title="Postal system">postal system</a> throughout the empire, based on several relay stations called <a href="/wiki/Chapar_Khaneh" title="Chapar Khaneh">Chapar Khaneh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Persepolis_Administrative_Archives" title="Persepolis Administrative Archives">Persepolis Administrative Archives</a> provide many insights into the Achaemenid government system. Found at <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> in 1930s, they are mostly in ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamite" title="Elamite">Elamite</a>; the remains of more than 10,000 of these cuneiform documents have been uncovered. <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> is represented by about 1,000 or more original records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[153]</a></sup> Only one tablet in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> has been identified so far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also many seals and seal impressions are found in these Persepolis archives. These documents represent administrative activity and flow of data in Persepolis over more than fifty consecutive years (509 to 457 BC).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Achaemenid_coinage">Achaemenid coinage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Achaemenid coinage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_coinage" title="Achaemenid coinage">Achaemenid coinage</a></div>
<p>The Persian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daric" title="Daric">daric</a> was the first <a href="/wiki/Gold_coin" title="Gold coin">gold coin</a> which, along with a similar silver coin, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siglos" title="Siglos">siglos</a>, introduced the bimetallic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monetary_standard" title="Monetary standard">monetary standard</a> of the Achaemenid Persian Empire which has continued till today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranica_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-iranica-161">[155]</a></sup> This was accomplished by <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a>, who reinforced the empire and expanded <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> as a ceremonial capital;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[156]</a></sup> he revolutionized the economy by placing it on the silver and gold coinage.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tax_districts">Tax districts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tax districts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Districts_of_the_Achaemenid_Empire" title="Districts of the Achaemenid Empire">Districts of the Achaemenid Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tribute_in_the_Achaemenid_Empire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Tribute_in_the_Achaemenid_Empire.jpg/330px-Tribute_in_the_Achaemenid_Empire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Tribute_in_the_Achaemenid_Empire.jpg/495px-Tribute_in_the_Achaemenid_Empire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Tribute_in_the_Achaemenid_Empire.jpg/660px-Tribute_in_the_Achaemenid_Empire.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tribute_in_the_Achaemenid_Empire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Districts_of_the_Achaemenid_Empire" title="Districts of the Achaemenid Empire">Volume of annual tribute per district</a>, in the Achaemenid Empire, according to <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HIII_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-HIII-163">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZA_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZA-164">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranica_-_Achaemenid_Relations_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iranica_-_Achaemenid_Relations-165">[159]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Darius also introduced a regulated and sustainable tax system that was precisely tailored to each satrapy, based on their supposed productivity and their economic potential. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> was assessed for the highest amount and for a startling mixture of commodities – 1,000 <a href="/wiki/Talent_(measurement)" title="Talent (measurement)">silver talents</a>, four months' supply of food for the army. <a href="/wiki/Hindush" title="Hindush">India</a> was clearly already fabled for its gold; <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> was known for the wealth of its crops; it was to be the granary of the Persian Empire (as later of Rome's) and was required to provide 120,000 measures of grain in addition to 700 talents of silver. This was exclusively a tax levied on subject peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historyworld.net_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-historyworld.net-166">[160]</a></sup> There is evidence that conquered and/or rebellious enemies could be sold into slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[161]</a></sup> Alongside its other innovations in administration and taxation, the Achaemenids may have been the first government in the ancient Near East to register private slave sales and tax them using an early form of <a href="/wiki/Sales_tax" title="Sales tax">sales tax</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abax_from_Darius_Vase.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="710" data-file-width="933" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Abax_from_Darius_Vase.jpg/220px-Abax_from_Darius_Vase.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Abax_from_Darius_Vase.jpg/330px-Abax_from_Darius_Vase.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Abax_from_Darius_Vase.jpg/440px-Abax_from_Darius_Vase.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abax_from_Darius_Vase.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Achaemenid tax collector, calculating on an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abax" title="Abax">Abax</a> or <a href="/wiki/Abacus" title="Abacus">Abacus</a>, according to the <a href="/wiki/Darius_Vase" title="Darius Vase">Darius Vase</a> (340–320 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[163]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Other accomplishments of Darius' reign included the codification of the <i>dāta</i> (a universal legal system which would become the basis of later Iranian law), and the construction of a new capital at <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation_and_communication">Transportation and communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Transportation and communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Under the Achaemenids, trade was extensive and there was an efficient infrastructure that facilitated the exchange of commodities in the far reaches of the empire. Tariffs on trade, along with agriculture and tribute, were major sources of revenue for the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historyworld.net_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-historyworld.net-166">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_IA_6F.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="3333" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_IA_6F.jpg/220px-Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_IA_6F.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_IA_6F.jpg/330px-Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_IA_6F.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_IA_6F.jpg/440px-Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_IA_6F.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_IA_6F.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Letter from the Satrap of Bactria to the governor of Khulmi, concerning camel keepers, 353 BC</div></div></div>
<p>The satrapies were linked by a 2,500-kilometer highway, the most impressive stretch being the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Road" title="Royal Road">Royal Road</a> from <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>, built by command of Darius I. It featured stations and <a href="/wiki/Caravanserai" title="Caravanserai">caravanserais</a> at specific intervals. The relays of mounted couriers (the <a href="/wiki/Angarium" title="Angarium">angarium</a>) could reach the remotest of areas in fifteen days. Herodotus observes that "there is nothing in the world that travels faster than these Persian couriers. Neither snow, nor rain, nor heat, nor gloom of night stays these courageous couriers from the swift completion of their appointed rounds."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[167]</a></sup> Despite the relative local independence afforded by the satrapy system, royal inspectors, the "eyes and ears of the king", toured the empire and reported on local conditions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Another highway of commerce was the <a href="/wiki/Khurasan_Road" title="Khurasan Road">Great Khorasan Road</a>, an informal mercantile route that originated in the fertile lowlands of Mesopotamia and snaked through the Zagros highlands, through the Iranian plateau and Afghanistan into the Central Asian regions of <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merv" title="Merv">Merv</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fergana_Valley" title="Fergana Valley">Ferghana</a>, allowing for the construction of frontier cities like <a href="/wiki/Cyropolis" title="Cyropolis">Cyropolis</a>. Following Alexander's conquests, this highway allowed for the spread of cultural syncretic fusions like <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">Greco-Buddhism</a> into Central Asia and China, as well as empires like the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">Indo-Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian</a> to profit from trade between East and West. This route was greatly rehabilitated and formalized during the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a>, during which it developed into a major component of the famed <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Despite its humble origins in Persis, the empire reached an enormous size under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>. Cyrus created a multi-state empire where he allowed regional rulers, called the "<a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a>", to rule as his proxy over a certain designated area of his empire called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satrapy" title="Satrapy">satrapy</a>. The basic rule of governance was based upon loyalty and obedience of each satrapy to the central power, or the king, and compliance with tax laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyv_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-cyv-175">[169]</a></sup> Due to the ethno-cultural diversity of the subject nations under the rule of Persia, its enormous geographic size, and the constant struggle for power by regional competitors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-book-22">[19]</a></sup> the creation of a professional army was necessary for both maintenance of the peace and to enforce the authority of the king in cases of rebellion and foreign threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmitt_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-schmitt-23">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant2002261_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant2002261-157">[151]</a></sup> Cyrus managed to create a strong land army, using it to advance in his campaigns in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_empire" title="Babylonian empire">Babylonia</a>, Lydia, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, which after his death was used by his son <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> against <a href="/wiki/Psamtik_III" title="Psamtik III">Psamtik III</a>. Cyrus would die battling a local Iranian insurgency in the empire, before he could have a chance to develop a naval force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[170]</a></sup> That task would fall to <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a>, who would officially give Persians their own royal navy to allow them to engage their enemies on multiple seas of this vast empire, from the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Sea" title="Ionian Sea">Ionian Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_composition">Military composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Military composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2335" data-file-width="4770" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg/330px-Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg/495px-Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg/660px-Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relief of throne-bearing soldiers in their native clothing at the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>, demonstrating the satrapies under his rule.</div></div></div>
<p>The empire's great armies were, like the empire itself, very diverse, having:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[g]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-84_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-84-179">[172]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedonians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_92-6"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-92">[86]</a></sup> European <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thracians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paionia" title="Paionia">Paeonians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, Achaean <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khuzistan" title="Khuzistan">Cissians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyrcanians" title="Hyrcanians">Hyrcanians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-62_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-62-180">[173]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldeans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-63_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-63-181">[174]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactrians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacae" title="Sacae">Sacae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-64_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-64-182">[175]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aria_(satrapy)" title="Aria (satrapy)">Arians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_Albania" title="Caucasian Albania">Caucasian Albanians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaumont_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaumont-183">[176]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khwarezm" title="Khwarezm">Chorasmians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandarians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daradas" title="Daradas">Dadicae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-66_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-66-184">[177]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Caspians" title="Caspians">Caspians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drangiana" title="Drangiana">Sarangae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashtun_people" title="Pashtun people">Pactyes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-67_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-67-185">[178]</a></sup> Utians, <a href="/wiki/Maka_(satrapy)" title="Maka (satrapy)">Mycians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yehud_Medinata" title="Yehud Medinata">Judeans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii=89_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii=89-186">[179]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_Cypriots" title="Greek Cypriots">Cyprians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-90_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-90-187">[180]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pamphylia" title="Pamphylia">Pamphylians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Lycians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorians" title="Dorians">Dorians</a> of Asia, <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Carians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Islands" title="Aegean Islands">Aegean islanders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aeolis" title="Aeolis">Aeolians</a>, Greeks from <a href="/wiki/Pontus_(region)" title="Pontus (region)">Pontus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balochistan_(Pakistan)" title="Balochistan (Pakistan)">Paricanians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-68_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-68-188">[181]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopians of Africa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-69_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-69-189">[182]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sistan_and_Baluchestan_Province" title="Sistan and Baluchestan Province">Ethiopians of Baluchistan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-70_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-70-190">[183]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libyans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-71_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-71-191">[184]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paphlagonia" title="Paphlagonia">Paphlagonians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kutaisi" href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-73_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-73-193">[186]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mysia" title="Mysia">Mysians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-74_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-74-194">[187]</a></sup> Asian <a href="/wiki/Bithyni" title="Bithyni">Thracians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-75_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-75-195">[188]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pisidia" title="Pisidia">Lasonii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Milyae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-77_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-77-196">[189]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mushki" title="Mushki">Moschi</a>, <a id="cite_ref-vii-78_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-78-197">[190]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">Mares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colchis" title="Colchis">Colchians</a>, <a id="cite_ref-vii=79_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii=79-198">[191]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> islanders,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-80_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-80-199">[192]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sagartians" title="Sagartians">Sagartians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-85_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-65-201">[194]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eordea" title="Eordea">Eordi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bottiaea" title="Bottiaea">Bottiaei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chalkidiki"
