Enienes, Dolopes, and <a href="/wiki/Magnesia_Prefecture" title="Magnesia Prefecture">Magnesians</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infantry">Infantry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Infantry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_king_killing_a_Greek_hoplite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="970" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Achaemenid_king_killing_a_Greek_hoplite.jpg/220px-Achaemenid_king_killing_a_Greek_hoplite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Achaemenid_king_killing_a_Greek_hoplite.jpg/330px-Achaemenid_king_killing_a_Greek_hoplite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Achaemenid_king_killing_a_Greek_hoplite.jpg/440px-Achaemenid_king_killing_a_Greek_hoplite.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_king_killing_a_Greek_hoplite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Achaemenid king killing a Greek <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">hoplite</a>. c. 500 BC–475 BC, at the time of <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>. <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_soldiers_against_Scythians.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="724" data-file-width="1098" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Achaemenid_soldiers_against_Scythians.jpg/220px-Achaemenid_soldiers_against_Scythians.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Achaemenid_soldiers_against_Scythians.jpg/330px-Achaemenid_soldiers_against_Scythians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Achaemenid_soldiers_against_Scythians.jpg/440px-Achaemenid_soldiers_against_Scythians.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_soldiers_against_Scythians.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Persian soldiers (left) fighting against Scythians. <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_seal" title="Cylinder seal">Cylinder seal</a> impression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[195]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The Achaemenid infantry consisted of three groups: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immortals_(Persian_Empire)" title="Immortals (Persian Empire)">Immortals</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sparabara" title="Sparabara">Sparabara</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Takabara" title="Takabara">Takabara</a>, though in the later years of the Achaemenid Empire, a fourth group, the <a href="/wiki/Cardaces" title="Cardaces">Cardaces</a>, were introduced.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immortals_(Persian_empire)" title="Immortals (Persian empire)">Immortals</a> were described by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> as being heavy <a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">infantry</a>, led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydarnes_II" title="Hydarnes II">Hydarnes</a>, that were kept constantly at a strength of exactly 10,000 men. He claimed that the unit's name stemmed from the custom that every killed, seriously wounded, or sick member was immediately replaced with a new one, maintaining the numbers and cohesion of the unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius.org2_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-livius.org2-203">[196]</a></sup> They had wicker shields, short spears, swords or large daggers, bow and arrow. Underneath their robes they wore <a href="/wiki/Scale_armour" title="Scale armour">scale armour</a> coats. The spear counterbalances of the common soldiery were of silver; to differentiate commanding ranks, the officers' spear butt-spikes were golden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius.org2_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-livius.org2-203">[196]</a></sup> Surviving Achaemenid coloured glazed bricks and carved reliefs represent the Immortals as wearing elaborate robes, hoop earrings and gold jewellery, though these garments and accessories were most likely worn only for ceremonial occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[197]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_infantry_on_Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="990" data-file-width="678" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Achaemenid_infantry_on_Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg/170px-Achaemenid_infantry_on_Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Achaemenid_infantry_on_Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg/255px-Achaemenid_infantry_on_Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Achaemenid_infantry_on_Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg/340px-Achaemenid_infantry_on_Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_infantry_on_Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Color reconstruction of Achaemenid infantry on the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Sarcophagus" title="Alexander Sarcophagus">Alexander Sarcophagus</a> (end of 4th century BC).</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sparabara" title="Sparabara">Sparabara</a> were usually the first to engage in hand-to-hand combat with the enemy. Although not much is known about them today, it is believed that they were the backbone of the Persian army who formed a <a href="/wiki/Shield_wall" title="Shield wall">shield wall</a> and used their two-metre-long spears to protect more vulnerable troops such as <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archers</a> from the enemy. The Sparabara were taken from the full members of Persian society, were trained from childhood to be soldiers and when not called out to fight on campaigns in distant lands they practised hunting on the vast plains of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>. However, when all was quiet and the Pax Persica held true, the Sparabara returned to normal life farming the land and grazing their herds. Because of this, they lacked true professional quality on the battlefield, yet they were well trained and courageous to the point of holding the line in most situations long enough for a counter-attack. They were armoured with quilted <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">linen</a> and carried large rectangular <a href="/wiki/Wicker" title="Wicker">wicker</a> shields as a form of light manoeuvrable defence. This, however, left them at a severe disadvantage against heavily armoured opponents such as the <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">hoplite</a>, and their two-metre-long spear was not able to give the Sparabara ample range to plausibly engage a trained <a href="/wiki/Phalanx" title="Phalanx">phalanx</a>. The wicker shields were able to effectively stop arrows but not strong enough to protect the soldier from spears. However, the Sparabara could deal with most other infantry, including trained units from the East.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Achaemenids relied heavily on <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archery</a>. Major contributing nations were the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elamites</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Composite_bow" title="Composite bow">composite bow</a> was used by the Persians and Medes, who adopted it from the Scythians and transmitted it to other nations, including the Greeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[198]</a></sup> The socketed, three-bladed (also known as trilobate or Scythian) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arrowheads" title="Arrowheads">arrowheads</a> made of copper alloy was the arrowhead variant normally used by the Achaemenid army. This variant required more expertise and precision to build.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[200]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Source says that these arrowheads were actually easier to make than iron varieties (July 2019)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Takabara" title="Takabara">Takabara</a> were a rare unit who were a tough type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peltasts" title="Peltasts">peltasts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[201]</a></sup> They tended to fight with their own native weapons which would have included a crescent-shaped light wickerwork <a href="/wiki/Shield" title="Shield">shield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Axe" title="Axe">axes</a> as well as light linen cloth and <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a>. The Takabara were recruited from territories that incorporated modern Iran.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cavalry">Cavalry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Cavalry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seal_of_Darius_the_Great_British_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1824" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Seal_of_Darius_the_Great_British_Museum.jpg/220px-Seal_of_Darius_the_Great_British_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Seal_of_Darius_the_Great_British_Museum.jpg/330px-Seal_of_Darius_the_Great_British_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Seal_of_Darius_the_Great_British_Museum.jpg/440px-Seal_of_Darius_the_Great_British_Museum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seal_of_Darius_the_Great_British_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seal of <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> hunting in a chariot, reading "I am Darius, the Great King" in Old Persian (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%90%8E%A0%F0%90%8E%AD%F0%90%8E%B6" title="wikt:𐎠𐎭𐎶">𐎠𐎭𐎶</a>𐏐<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%90%8E%AD%F0%90%8E%A0%F0%90%8E%BC%F0%90%8E%B9%F0%90%8E%BA%F0%90%8E%A2%F0%90%8F%81%F0%90%8E%B4" title="wikt:𐎭𐎠𐎼𐎹𐎺𐎢𐏁𐎴">𐎭𐎠𐎼𐎹𐎺𐎢𐏁𐎴</a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%90%8F%8B" title="wikt:𐏋">𐏋</a>, "<i>adam Dārayavaʰuš xšāyaθiya</i>"), as well as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamite" title="Elamite">Elamite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Babylonian</a>. The word "great" only appears in Babylonian. <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[203]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The armoured Persian horsemen and their death dealing chariots were invincible. No man dared face them</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>The Persian cavalry was crucial for conquering nations and maintained its importance in the Achaemenid army to the last days of the Achaemenid Empire. The cavalry were separated into four groups. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chariot_archer" title="Chariot archer">chariot archers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_cavalry" title="Horse cavalry">horse cavalry</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Camel_cavalry" title="Camel cavalry">camel cavalry</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Persian_war_elephants" title="Persian war elephants">war elephants</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_cavalry_in_Asia_Minor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1509" data-file-width="1826" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Achaemenid_cavalry_in_Asia_Minor.jpg/220px-Achaemenid_cavalry_in_Asia_Minor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Achaemenid_cavalry_in_Asia_Minor.jpg/330px-Achaemenid_cavalry_in_Asia_Minor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Achaemenid_cavalry_in_Asia_Minor.jpg/440px-Achaemenid_cavalry_in_Asia_Minor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_cavalry_in_Asia_Minor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Achaemenid cavalryman in the satrapy of <a href="/wiki/Hellespontine_Phrygia" title="Hellespontine Phrygia">Hellespontine Phrygia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alt%C4%B1kula%C3%A7_Sarcophagus" title="Altıkulaç Sarcophagus">Altıkulaç Sarcophagus</a>, early 4th century BC.</div></div></div>
<p>In the later years of the Achaemenid Empire, the chariot archer had become merely a ceremonial part of the Persian army, yet in the early years of the Empire, their use was widespread. The chariot archers were armed with spears, bows, arrows, swords, and <a href="/wiki/Scale_armour" title="Scale armour">scale armour</a>. The horses were also suited with scale armour similar to scale armour of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanian" title="Sassanian">Sassanian</a> <a href="/wiki/Cataphract" title="Cataphract">cataphracts</a>. The chariots would contain imperial symbols and decorations.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alt%C4%B1kula%C3%A7_Sarcophagus_Combat_scene_(detail).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2647" data-file-width="4182" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Alt%C4%B1kula%C3%A7_Sarcophagus_Combat_scene_%28detail%29.jpg/220px-Alt%C4%B1kula%C3%A7_Sarcophagus_Combat_scene_%28detail%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Alt%C4%B1kula%C3%A7_Sarcophagus_Combat_scene_%28detail%29.jpg/330px-Alt%C4%B1kula%C3%A7_Sarcophagus_Combat_scene_%28detail%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Alt%C4%B1kula%C3%A7_Sarcophagus_Combat_scene_%28detail%29.jpg/440px-Alt%C4%B1kula%C3%A7_Sarcophagus_Combat_scene_%28detail%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alt%C4%B1kula%C3%A7_Sarcophagus_Combat_scene_(detail).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Armoured cavalry: Achaemenid Dynast of <a href="/wiki/Hellespontine_Phrygia" title="Hellespontine Phrygia">Hellespontine Phrygia</a> attacking a Greek <a href="/wiki/Psiloi" title="Psiloi">psiloi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alt%C4%B1kula%C3%A7_Sarcophagus" title="Altıkulaç Sarcophagus">Altıkulaç Sarcophagus</a>, early 4th century BC.</div></div></div>
<p>The horses used by the Achaemenids for cavalry were often suited with scale armour, like most cavalry units. The riders often had the same armour as Infantry units, wicker shields, short spears, swords or large daggers, bow and arrow, and scale armour coats. The camel cavalry was different, because the camels and sometimes the riders, were provided little protection against enemies, yet when they were offered protection, they would have spears, swords, bow, arrow, and scale armour. The camel cavalry was first introduced into the Persian army by <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thymbra" title="Battle of Thymbra">Battle of Thymbra</a>. The elephant was most likely introduced into the Persian army by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a> after his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid_invasion_of_the_Indus_Valley" title="Achaemenid invasion of the Indus Valley">conquest of the Indus Valley</a>. They may have been used in Greek campaigns by Darius and <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>, but Greek accounts only mention 15 of them being used at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Battle of Gaugamela</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navy">Navy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Navy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_navy" title="Achaemenid navy">Achaemenid navy</a></div>
<p>Since its foundation by Cyrus, the Persian empire had been primarily a land empire with a strong army, but void of any actual naval forces. By the 5th century BC, this was to change, as the empire came across Greek and Egyptian forces, each with their own maritime traditions and capabilities. <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> (Darius I) was the first Achaemenid king to invest in a Persian fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-navy-211">[204]</a></sup> Even by then no true "imperial navy" had existed either in Greece or Egypt. Persia would become the first empire, under Darius, to inaugurate and deploy the first regular imperial navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-navy-211">[204]</a></sup> Despite this achievement, the personnel for the imperial navy would not come from Iran, but were often <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> (mostly from <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a>), <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_people" title="Greek people">Greeks</a> chosen by Darius the Great to operate the empire's combat vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_211-2"><a href="#cite_note-navy-211">[204]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BeachedShipsMarathon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="351" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/BeachedShipsMarathon.jpg/220px-BeachedShipsMarathon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/BeachedShipsMarathon.jpg/330px-BeachedShipsMarathon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/BeachedShipsMarathon.jpg/440px-BeachedShipsMarathon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BeachedShipsMarathon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstitution of Persian landing ships at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">Battle of Marathon</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>At first the ships were built in Sidon by the Phoenicians; the first Achaemenid ships measured about 40 meters in length and 6 meters in width, able to transport up to 300 Persian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Immortals" title="Persian Immortals">troops</a> at any one trip. Soon, other states of the empire were constructing their own ships, each incorporating slight local preferences. The ships eventually found their way to the Persian Gulf<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_211-3"><a href="#cite_note-navy-211">[204]</a></sup> and Persian naval forces laid the foundation for a strong Persian maritime presence there. Persians also had ships often of 100 to 200 capacity patrolling the empire's various rivers including the <a href="/wiki/Karun" title="Karun">Karun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> in the west, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_211-4"><a href="#cite_note-navy-211">[204]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ship_dashed_against_ship,_till_the_Persian_Army_dead_strewed_the_deep_like_flowers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2453" data-file-width="1750" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ship_dashed_against_ship%2C_till_the_Persian_Army_dead_strewed_the_deep_like_flowers.jpg/170px-Ship_dashed_against_ship%2C_till_the_Persian_Army_dead_strewed_the_deep_like_flowers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ship_dashed_against_ship%2C_till_the_Persian_Army_dead_strewed_the_deep_like_flowers.jpg/255px-Ship_dashed_against_ship%2C_till_the_Persian_Army_dead_strewed_the_deep_like_flowers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ship_dashed_against_ship%2C_till_the_Persian_Army_dead_strewed_the_deep_like_flowers.jpg/340px-Ship_dashed_against_ship%2C_till_the_Persian_Army_dead_strewed_the_deep_like_flowers.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ship_dashed_against_ship,_till_the_Persian_Army_dead_strewed_the_deep_like_flowers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greek ships against Achaemenid ships at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Achaemenid navy established bases located along the Karun, and in Bahrain, Oman, and Yemen. The Persian fleet was not only used for peace-keeping purposes along the Karun but also opened the door to trade with India via the Persian Gulf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_211-5"><a href="#cite_note-navy-211">[204]</a></sup> Darius's navy was in many ways a world power at the time, but it would be <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a> who in the summer of 397 BC would build a formidable navy, as part of a rearmament which would lead to his decisive victory at <a href="/wiki/Knidos" title="Knidos">Knidos</a> in 394 BC, re-establishing Achaemenid power in <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>. Artaxerxes II would also utilize his navy to later on quell a rebellion in Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navi_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-navi-212">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>The construction material of choice was wood, but some armoured Achaemenid ships had metallic blades on the front, often meant to slice enemy ships using the ship's momentum. Naval ships were also equipped with hooks on the side to grab enemy ships, or to negotiate their position. The ships were propelled by sails or manpower. The ships the Persians created were unique. As far as maritime engagement, the ships were equipped with two <a href="/wiki/Mangonel" title="Mangonel">mangonels</a> that would launch projectiles such as stones, or flammable substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_211-6"><a href="#cite_note-navy-211">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> describes his eyewitness account of a massive military bridge created by joining 37 Persian ships across the Tigris. The Persians utilized each boat's buoyancy, in order to support a connected bridge above which supply could be transferred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_211-7"><a href="#cite_note-navy-211">[204]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> also gives many accounts of Persians utilizing ships to build bridges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[207]</a></sup>
</p><p>Darius the Great, in an attempt to subdue the <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythian</a> horsemen north of the Black Sea, crossed over at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosphorus" title="Bosphorus">Bosphorus</a>, using an enormous bridge made by connecting Achaemenid boats, then marched up to the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>, crossing it by means of a second boat bridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[208]</a></sup> The bridge over the Bosphorus essentially connected the nearest tip of Asia to Europe, encompassing at least some 1000 meters of open water if not more. Herodotus describes the spectacle, and calls it the "bridge of Darius":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>"<i>Strait called Bosphorus, across which the bridge of Darius had been thrown, is hundred and twenty <a href="/wiki/Furlong" title="Furlong">furlongs</a> in length, reaching from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euxine" title="Euxine">Euxine</a>, to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propontis" title="Propontis">Propontis</a>. The Propontis is five hundred furlongs across and fourteen hundred long. Its waters flow into the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Hellespont</a>, the length of which is four hundred furlongs ...</i>"</dd></dl>
<p>Years later, a similar boat bridge would be constructed by Xerxes the Great (<a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>), in his invasion of Greece. Although the Persians failed to capture the Greek city-states completely, the tradition of maritime involvement was carried down by the Persian kings, most notably Artaxerxes II. Years later, when Alexander invaded Persia and during his advancement into India, he took a page from the Persian art of war, by having <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perdiccas" title="Perdiccas">Perdiccas</a> construct a similar boat-bridge at the Indus river, in India in the spring of 327 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[210]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:344px;max-width:344px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:200px;max-width:200px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:137px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corner_of_the_Apadana_Darius_the_Great_inscription.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Corner_of_the_Apadana_Darius_the_Great_inscription.jpg/198px-Corner_of_the_Apadana_Darius_the_Great_inscription.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Corner_of_the_Apadana_Darius_the_Great_inscription.jpg/297px-Corner_of_the_Apadana_Darius_the_Great_inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Corner_of_the_Apadana_Darius_the_Great_inscription.jpg/396px-Corner_of_the_Apadana_Darius_the_Great_inscription.jpg 2x" width="198"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Gold foundation tablets of Darius I for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apadana_Palace" title="Apadana Palace">Apadana Palace</a>, in their original stone box. The <a href="/wiki/Apadana_hoard" title="Apadana hoard">Apadana coin hoard</a> had been deposited underneath c. 510 BC.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:140px;max-width:140px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:137px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deposition_plate_of_Darius_I_in_Persepolis.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="958" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Deposition_plate_of_Darius_I_in_Persepolis.jpg/138px-Deposition_plate_of_Darius_I_in_Persepolis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Deposition_plate_of_Darius_I_in_Persepolis.jpg/207px-Deposition_plate_of_Darius_I_in_Persepolis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Deposition_plate_of_Darius_I_in_Persepolis.jpg/276px-Deposition_plate_of_Darius_I_in_Persepolis.jpg 2x" width="138"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">One of the two gold deposition plates. Two more were in silver. They all had the same trilingual inscription (DPh inscription).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[211]</a></sup></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>During the reign of Cyrus and Darius, and as long as the seat of government was still at <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a>, the language of the chancellery was <a href="/wiki/Elamite_language" title="Elamite language">Elamite</a>. This is primarily attested in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persepolis_Fortification_Tablets" title="Persepolis Fortification Tablets">Persepolis fortification and treasury tablets</a> that reveal details of the day-to-day functioning of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIR_Dandamayev_Elamite_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIR_Dandamayev_Elamite-219">[212]</a></sup> In the grand rock-face inscriptions of the kings, the Elamite texts are always accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> (Babylonian dialect) and <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> inscriptions, and it appears that in these cases, the Elamite texts are translations of the Old Persian ones. It is then likely that although Elamite was used by the capital government in Susa, it was not a standardized language of government everywhere in the empire. The use of Elamite is not attested after 458 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[213]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BehistunInscriptiondetail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="841" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/BehistunInscriptiondetail.jpg/220px-BehistunInscriptiondetail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/BehistunInscriptiondetail.jpg/330px-BehistunInscriptiondetail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/BehistunInscriptiondetail.jpg/440px-BehistunInscriptiondetail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BehistunInscriptiondetail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A section of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Persian_language" title="Old Persian language">Old Persian</a> part of the trilingual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behistun_inscription" title="Behistun inscription">Behistun inscription</a>. Other versions are in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_language" title="Babylonian language">Babylonian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elamite_language" title="Elamite language">Elamite</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aramaic_translation_of_the_behistun_inscripton.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="705" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Aramaic_translation_of_the_behistun_inscripton.png/220px-Aramaic_translation_of_the_behistun_inscripton.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Aramaic_translation_of_the_behistun_inscripton.png/330px-Aramaic_translation_of_the_behistun_inscripton.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Aramaic_translation_of_the_behistun_inscripton.png/440px-Aramaic_translation_of_the_behistun_inscripton.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aramaic_translation_of_the_behistun_inscripton.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A copy of the Behistun inscription in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Official_Aramaic" title="Official Aramaic">Aramaic</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>. Aramaic was the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of the empire.</div></div></div>
<p>Following the conquest of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Official_Aramaic" title="Official Aramaic">Aramaic</a> language (as used in that territory) was adopted as a "vehicle for written communication between the different regions of the vast empire with its different peoples and languages. The use of a single official language, which modern scholarship has dubbed "Official Aramaic" or "Imperial Aramaic", can be assumed to have greatly contributed to the astonishing success of the Achaemenids in holding their far-flung empire together for as long as they did."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[214]</a></sup> In 1955, Richard Frye questioned the classification of Imperial Aramaic as an "<a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a>", noting that no surviving edict expressly and unambiguously accorded that status to any particular language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[215]</a></sup> Frye reclassifies Imperial Aramaic as the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of the Achaemenid territories, suggesting then that the Achaemenid-era use of Aramaic was more pervasive than generally thought. Many centuries after the fall of the empire, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic_script" title="Aramaic script">Aramaic script</a> and—as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huzvarishn" title="Huzvarishn">ideograms</a>—Aramaic vocabulary would survive as the essential characteristics of the <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_scripts" title="Pahlavi scripts">Pahlavi writing system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Old Persian also appears on some seals and art objects, that language is attested primarily in the Achaemenid inscriptions of Western Iran, suggesting then that Old Persian was the common language of that region. However, by the reign of Artaxerxes II, the grammar and orthography of the inscriptions was so "far from perfect"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[217]</a></sup> that it has been suggested that the scribes who composed those texts had already largely forgotten the language, and had to rely on older inscriptions, which they to a great extent reproduced verbatim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the occasion demanded, Achaemenid administrative correspondence was conducted in <a href="/wiki/Old_Greek" title="Old Greek">Greek</a>, making it a widely used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bureaucratic" title="Bureaucratic">bureaucratic</a> language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranian,_E._Tucker_2001_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iranian,_E._Tucker_2001-9">[8]</a></sup> Even though the Achaemenids had extensive contacts with the Greeks and vice versa, and had conquered many of the Greek-speaking areas both in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> during different periods of the empire, the native Old Iranian sources provide no indication of Greek linguistic evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranian,_E._Tucker_2001_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Iranian,_E._Tucker_2001-9">[8]</a></sup> However, there is plenty of evidence (in addition to the accounts of Herodotus) that Greeks, apart from being deployed and employed in the core regions of the empire, also evidently lived and worked in the heartland of the Achaemenid Empire, namely Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranian,_E._Tucker_2001_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Iranian,_E._Tucker_2001-9">[8]</a></sup> For example, Greeks were part of the various ethnicities that constructed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius%27_palace_in_Susa" title="Darius' palace in Susa">Darius' palace in Susa</a>, apart from the Greek inscriptions found nearby there, and one short Persepolis tablet written in Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranian,_E._Tucker_2001_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-Iranian,_E._Tucker_2001-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Customs">Customs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Customs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em 0;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-empty-cell{border:none;padding:0;width:1px}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-image-div{width:52px}html.client-js body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2015</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1688" data-file-width="2260" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg/220px-Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg/330px-Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg/440px-Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Persian_Lion_Rhyton" title="Achaemenid Persian Lion Rhyton">Achaemenid rhyton</a>, or drinking vessel</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> mentions that the Persians were invited to great <a href="/wiki/Birthday" title="Birthday">birthday</a> feasts (Herodotus, <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">Histories</a></i> 8), which would be followed by many desserts, a treat which they reproached the Greeks for omitting from their meals. He also observed that the Persians drank wine in large quantities and used it even for counsel, deliberating on important affairs when drunk, and deciding the next day, when sober, whether to act on the decision or set it aside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[219]</a></sup> Bowing to superiors or royalty was one of the many Persian customs adopted by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mithra" title="Mithra">Mithra</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malandra1983_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-Malandra1983-227">[220]</a></sup> was the most widely worshipped and followed deity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briant2002_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Briant2002-228">[221]</a></sup> in the Achaemenid Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dandamaev1989_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dandamaev1989-229">[222]</a></sup> His temples and symbols were the most widespread,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bivar1998_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bivar1998-230">[223]</a></sup> most people bore names related to him<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AdrychBraceyDalglish2017_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-AdrychBraceyDalglish2017-231">[224]</a></sup> and most festivals were dedicated to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perrot2013_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perrot2013-232">[225]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StojanovStoyanov2000_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-StojanovStoyanov2000-233">[226]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_toleration" title="Religious toleration">Religious toleration</a> has been described as a "remarkable feature" of the Achaemenid Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-234">[227]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> reports that king <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> released the Jews from their <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_captivity" title="Babylonian captivity">Babylonian captivity</a> in 539–530 BC, and permitted them to return to their homeland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[228]</a></sup> Cyrus the Great assisted in the restoration of the sacred places of various cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-234">[227]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was during the Achaemenid period that <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> reached South-Western Iran, where it came to be accepted by the rulers and through them became a defining element of Persian culture. The religion was not only accompanied by a formalization of the concepts and divinities of the traditional Iranian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a> but also introduced several novel ideas, including that of <a href="/wiki/Free_will_in_theology" title="Free will in theology">free will</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[230]</a></sup> Under the patronage of the Achaemenid kings, and by the 5th century BC as the <i>de facto</i> religion of the state, Zoroastrianism reached all corners of the empire.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:201312_iran_Shiraz_72_(12475982935).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/201312_iran_Shiraz_72_%2812475982935%29.jpg/220px-201312_iran_Shiraz_72_%2812475982935%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/201312_iran_Shiraz_72_%2812475982935%29.jpg/330px-201312_iran_Shiraz_72_%2812475982935%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/201312_iran_Shiraz_72_%2812475982935%29.jpg/440px-201312_iran_Shiraz_72_%2812475982935%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:201312_iran_Shiraz_72_(12475982935).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bas-relief of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farvahar" title="Farvahar">Farvahar</a> at Persepolis</div></div></div>
<p>During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_I" title="Artaxerxes I">Artaxerxes I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Darius_II" title="Darius II">Darius II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> wrote "[the Persians] have no images of the gods, no temples nor altars, and consider the use of them a sign of folly. This comes, I think, from their not believing the gods to have the same nature with men, as the Greeks imagine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodotus,_I.131_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herodotus,_I.131-238">[231]</a></sup> He claims the Persians offer sacrifice to: "the sun and moon, to the earth, to fire, to water, and to the winds. These are the only gods whose worship has come down to them from ancient times. At a later period, they began the worship of <a href="/wiki/Urania" title="Urania">Urania</a>, which they borrowed from the Arabians and Assyrians. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mylitta" title="Mylitta">Mylitta</a> is the name by which the Assyrians know this <a href="/wiki/Goddess" title="Goddess">goddess</a>, to whom the Persians referred as <a href="/wiki/Anahita" title="Anahita">Anahita</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodotus,_I.131_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-Herodotus,_I.131-238">[231]</a></sup> (The original name here is <a href="/wiki/Mithra" title="Mithra">Mithra</a>, which has since been explained to be a confusion of Anahita with Mithra, understandable since they were commonly worshipped together in one temple).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Babylonian scholar-priest <a href="/wiki/Berossus" title="Berossus">Berosus</a> records—although writing over seventy years after the reign of <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II Mnemon</a>— that the emperor had been the first to make <a href="/wiki/Cult" title="Cult">cult</a> statues of divinities and have them placed in temples in many of the major cities of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[232]</a></sup> Berosus also substantiates Herodotus when he says the Persians knew of no images of gods until Artaxerxes II erected those images. On the means of sacrifice, Herodotus adds "they raise no altar, light no fire, pour no libations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodotus,_I.132_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herodotus,_I.132-240">[233]</a></sup> This sentence has been interpreted to identify a critical (but later) accretion to Zoroastrianism. An altar with a wood-burning fire and the <a href="/wiki/Yasna" title="Yasna">Yasna</a> service at which libations are poured are all clearly identifiable with modern Zoroastrianism, but apparently, were practices that had not yet developed in the mid-5th century. Boyce also assigns that development to the reign of Artaxerxes II (4th century BC), as an orthodox response to the innovation of the shrine cults.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Herodotus also observed that "no prayer or offering can be made without a <a href="/wiki/Magi" title="Magi">magus</a> present"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodotus,_I.132_240-1"><a href="#cite_note-Herodotus,_I.132-240">[233]</a></sup> but this should not be confused with what is today understood by the term <i>magus</i>, that is a <i>magupat</i> (modern Persian: <i>mobed</i>), a Zoroastrian priest. Nor does Herodotus' description of the term as one of the tribes or castes of the Medes necessarily imply that these <i>magi</i> were Medians. They simply were a hereditary priesthood to be found all over Western Iran and although (originally) not associated with any one specific religion, they were traditionally responsible for all ritual and religious services. Although the unequivocal identification of the <i>magus</i> with Zoroastrianism came later (Sassanid era, 3rd–7th century AD), it is from Herodotus' <i>magus</i> of the mid-5th century that Zoroastrianism was subject to doctrinal modifications that are today considered to be revocations of the original teachings of the prophet. Also, many of the ritual practices described in the <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Vendidad" title="Vendidad">Vendidad</a></i> (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exposure_of_the_dead_(Zoroastrianism)" title="Exposure of the dead (Zoroastrianism)">exposure of the dead</a>) were already practised by the <i>magu</i> of Herodotus' time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women_in_the_Achaemenid_Empire">Women in the Achaemenid Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Women in the Achaemenid Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The position of women in the Achaemenid Empire differed depending on which culture they belonged to and therefore varied depending on the region. The position of Persian women in actual Persia has traditionally been described from mythological Biblical references and the sometimes biased Ancient Greek sources, neither of them fully reliable as sources, but the most reliable reference are the archeological Persepolis Fortification Tablets (PFT), which describes women in connection to the royal court in Persepolis, from royal women to female laborers who were recipients of food rations at Persepolis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>The hierarchy of the royal women at the Persian court was ranked with the king's mother first, followed by the queen and the king's daughters, the king's concubines, and the other women of the royal palace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>  The king normally married a female member of the royal family or a Persian noblewoman related to a satrap or another important Persian man; it was permitted for members of the royal family to marry relatives, but there is no evidence for marriage between closer family members than half-siblings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-2"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>  The king's concubines were often either slaves, sometimes prisoners of war, or foreign princesses, whom the king did not marry because they were foreigners, and whose children did not have the right to inherit the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-3"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>Greek sources accuse the king of having hundreds of concubines secluded in a <a href="/wiki/Harem" title="Harem">harem</a>, but there is no archeological evidence supporting the existence of a harem, or the seclusion of women from contact with men, at the Persian court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-4"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>  The royal women joined the king at breakfast and dinner and accompanied him on his journeys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-5"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>  They may have participated in the royal hunt, as well as during the royal banquets; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herodotos" title="Herodotos">Herodotus</a> relates how the Persian envoys at the Macedonian court demanded the presence of women during a banquet because it was the custom for women to participate in the banquets in their own country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-6"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>  The queen may have attended the king's audience, and archeological evidence shows that she gave her own audiences, at least for female supplicants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-7"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>  Royal women and noblewomen at court could furthermore travel on their own, accompanied by both male and female staff, own and manage their own fortune, land, and business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-8"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>  Depictions of Persian women show them with long dresses and veils which did not cover their faces nor their hair, only flowing down over their neck at the back of the head as an ornament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-9"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>Royal and aristocratic Achaemenid women were given an education in subjects that did not appear compatible with seclusions, such as horsemanship and archery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[236]</a></sup>   Royal and aristocratic women held and managed vast estates and workshops and employed large numbers of servants and professional laborers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[237]</a></sup> Royal and aristocratic women do not seem to have lived in seclusion from men, since it is known that they appeared in public<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brosius,_Maria_1996._pp._83-93_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brosius,_Maria_1996._pp._83-93-245">[238]</a></sup> and traveled with their husbands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brosius,_Maria_1996._pp._83-93_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brosius,_Maria_1996._pp._83-93-245">[238]</a></sup> participated in hunting<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[239]</a></sup> and in feasts:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[240]</a></sup>  at least the chief wife of a royal or aristocratic man did not live in seclusion, as it is clearly stated that wives customarily accompanied their husbands at dinner banquets, although they left the banquet when the "women entertainers" came in and the men began "merrymaking".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[241]</a></sup>
</p><p>No woman ever ruled the Achaemenid Empire, as <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarch</a> or as <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a>, but some queens consort are known to have had influence over the affairs of state, notably <a href="/wiki/Atossa" title="Atossa">Atossa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parysatis" title="Parysatis">Parysatis</a>.
</p><p>There are no evidence of any women being employed as an official in the administration or within religious service, however, there are plenty of archeological evidence of women being employed as free labourers in Persepolis, where they worked alongside men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-10"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>  Women could be employed as the leaders of their workforce, known by the title <i>arraššara pašabena</i>, which were then given a higher salary than the male workers of their workforce;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-11"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup> and while female laborers were given less than men, qualified workers within the crafts were given equal pay regardless of their sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maria_Brosius_241-12"><a href="#cite_note-Maria_Brosius-241">[234]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_architecture" title="Achaemenid architecture">Achaemenid architecture</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2015</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Achaemenid architecture included large cities, temples, palaces, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mausoleums" title="Mausoleums">mausoleums</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Cyrus" title="Tomb of Cyrus">tomb of Cyrus</a> the Great. The quintessential feature of Persian architecture was its eclectic nature with elements of Median, Assyrian, and Asiatic Greek all incorporated, yet maintaining a unique Persian identity seen in the finished products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[242]</a></sup> Its influence pervades the regions ruled by the Achaemenids, from the Mediterranean shores to India, especially with its emphasis on monumental stone-cut design and gardens subdivided by water-courses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>Achaemenid art includes <a href="/wiki/Frieze" title="Frieze">frieze</a> reliefs, metalwork such as the <a href="/wiki/Oxus_Treasure" title="Oxus Treasure">Oxus Treasure</a>, decoration of palaces, glazed brick masonry, fine craftsmanship (masonry, carpentry, etc.), and gardening. Although the Persians took artists, with their styles and techniques, from all corners of their empire, they produced not simply a combination of styles, but a synthesis of a new unique Persian style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[244]</a></sup> Cyrus the Great in fact had an extensive ancient Iranian heritage behind him; the rich Achaemenid gold work, which inscriptions suggest may have been a specialty of the Medes, was for instance in the tradition of the delicate metalwork found in <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> II times at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hasanlu" title="Hasanlu">Hasanlu</a> and still earlier at <a href="/wiki/Marlik" title="Marlik">Marlik</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>One of the most remarkable examples of both Achaemenid architecture and art is the grand palace of <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, and its detailed workmanship, coupled with its grand scale. In describing the construction of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palace_at_Susa" title="Palace at Susa">palace at Susa</a>, Darius the Great records that:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Yaka timber was brought from <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandara</a> and from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carmania_(satrapy)" title="Carmania (satrapy)">Carmania</a>. The gold was brought from <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> ... the precious stone lapis-lazuli and carnelian ... was brought from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a>. The turquoise from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chorasmia" title="Chorasmia">Chorasmia</a>, the silver and ebony from <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, the ornamentation from <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>, the ivory from <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Arachosia" title="Arachosia">Arachosia</a>. The stone-cutters who wrought the stone were Ionians and <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardians</a>. The goldsmiths were <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a>. The men who wrought the wood were Sardinians and Egyptians. The men who wrought the baked brick were Babylonians. The men who adorned the wall, those were Medes and Egyptians.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p></blockquote>
<p>This was imperial art on a scale the world had not seen before. Materials and artists were drawn from all corners of the empire, and thus tastes, styles, and motifs became mixed together in an eclectic art and architecture that in itself mirrored the Persian empire.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><br/>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
