<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tombs">Tombs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Tombs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1192" data-file-width="958" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg/170px-Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg/255px-Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg/340px-Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb of <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a> in <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a></div></div></div>
<p>Many Achaemenid rulers built tombs for themselves. The most famous, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naqsh-e_Rustam" title="Naqsh-e Rustam">Naqsh-e Rustam</a>, is an ancient necropolis located about 12 km north-west of <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, with the tombs of four of the kings of the dynasty carved in this mountain: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Darius_I" title="Tomb of Darius I">Darius I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_I" title="Artaxerxes I">Artaxerxes I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Darius_II" title="Darius II">Darius II</a>. Other kings constructed their own tombs elsewhere. <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a> preferred to carve their tombs beside their spring capital <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, the left tomb belonging to <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a> and the right tomb belonging to <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a>, the last Achaemenid king to have a tomb. The tomb of the founder of the Achaemenid dynasty, <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>, was built in <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a> (now a world heritage site).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg/220px-The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg/330px-The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg/440px-The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_at_Halicarnassus" title="Mausoleum at Halicarnassus">Mausoleum at Halicarnassus</a>, one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_wonders_of_the_ancient_world" title="Seven wonders of the ancient world">Seven wonders of the ancient world</a>, was built by Greek architects for the local Persian <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> of <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mausolus" title="Mausolus">Mausolus</a> (Scale model)</div></div></div>
<p>The Achaemenid Empire left a lasting impression on the heritage and cultural identity of Asia, Europe, and the Middle East, and influenced the development and structure of future <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">empires</a>. In fact, the Greeks, and later on the Romans, adopted the best features of the Persian method of governing an empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[245]</a></sup> The Persian model of governance was particularly formative in the expansion and maintenance of the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a>, whose rule is widely considered the period of the '<a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>'. Like the ancient Persians, the Abbasid dynasty centered their vast empire in Mesopotamia (at the newly founded cities of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samarra" title="Samarra">Samarra</a>, close to the historical site of Babylon), derived much of their support from Persian aristocracy and heavily incorporated the Persian language and architecture into Islamic culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hovannisian_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hovannisian-253">[246]</a></sup>
The Achaemenid Empire is noted in Western history as the antagonist of the <a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">Greek city-states</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a> and for the emancipation of the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_captivity" title="Babylonian captivity">Jewish exiles in Babylon</a>. The historical mark of the empire went far beyond its territorial and military influences and included cultural, social, technological and religious influences as well. For example, many <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athenians</a> adopted Achaemenid customs in their daily lives in a reciprocal cultural exchange,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[247]</a></sup> some being employed by or allied to the Persian kings. The impact of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyrus%27s_edict" title="Cyrus's edict">Cyrus's edict</a> is mentioned in Judeo-Christian texts, and the empire was instrumental in the spread of <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> as far east as China. The empire also set the tone for the <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>, heritage and <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (also known as Persia).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[248]</a></sup>
Historian <a href="/wiki/Arnold_J._Toynbee" title="Arnold J. Toynbee">Arnold Toynbee</a> regarded Abbasid society as a "reintegration" or "reincarnation" of Achaemenid society, as the synthesis of Persian, Turkic and Islamic modes of governance and knowledge allowed for the spread of <a href="/wiki/Persianate_society" title="Persianate society">Persianate culture</a> over a wide swath of Eurasia through the Turkic-origin <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">Seljuq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safavid_Iran" title="Safavid Iran">Safavid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal</a> empires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hovannisian_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hovannisian-253">[246]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Lewis" title="Bernard Lewis">Bernard Lewis</a> wrote that
</p>
<blockquote><p>The work of Iranians can be seen in every field of cultural endeavor, including Arabic poetry, to which poets of Iranian origin composing their poems in Arabic made a very significant contribution. In a sense, Iranian Islam is a second advent of Islam itself, a new Islam sometimes referred to as Islam-i-Ajam. It was this Persian Islam, rather than the original Arab Islam, that was brought to new areas and new peoples: to the Turks, first in Central Asia and then in the Middle East in the country which came to be called Turkey, and of course to India. The Ottoman Turks brought a form of Iranian civilization to the walls of Vienna. [...] By the time of the great Mongol invasions of the thirteenth century, Iranian Islam had become not only an important component; it had become a dominant element in Islam itself, and for several centuries the main centers of the Islamic power and civilization were in countries that were, if not Iranian, at least marked by Iranian civilization ... The major centers of Islam in the late medieval and early modern periods, the centers of both political and cultural power, such as India, Central Asia, Iran, Turkey, were all part of this Iranian civilization.</p></blockquote>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georg_W._F._Hegel" title="Georg W. F. Hegel">Georg W. F. Hegel</a> in his work <i><a href="/wiki/Lectures_on_the_Philosophy_of_History" title="Lectures on the Philosophy of History">The Philosophy of History</a></i> introduces the Persian Empire as the "first empire that passed away" and its people as the "first historical people" in history. According to his account;
</p>
<blockquote><p>The Persian Empire is an empire in the modern sense—like that which existed in Germany, and the great imperial realm under the sway of Napoleon; for we find it consisting of a number of states, which are indeed dependent, but which have retained their own individuality, their manners, and laws. The general enactments, binding upon all, did not infringe upon their political and social idiosyncrasies, but even protected and maintained them; so that each of the nations that constitute the whole, had its own form of constitution. As light illuminates everything—imparting to each object a peculiar vitality—so the Persian Empire extends over a multitude of nations, and leaves to each one its particular character. Some have even kings of their own; each one its distinct language, arms, way of life and customs. All this diversity coexists harmoniously under the impartial dominion of Light ... a combination of peoples—leaving each of them free. Thereby, a stop is put to that barbarism and ferocity with which the nations had been wont to carry on their destructive feuds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[249]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Will_Durant" title="Will Durant">Will Durant</a>, the American historian and philosopher, during one of his speeches, "Persia in the History of Civilization", as an address before the <i><a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93America_Society" title="Iran–America Society">Iran–America Society</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a> on 21 April 1948, stated:
</p>
<blockquote><p>For thousands of years Persians have been creating beauty. Sixteen centuries before Christ there went from these regions or near it ... You have been here a kind of watershed of civilization, pouring your blood and thought and art and religion eastward and westward into the world ... I need not rehearse for you again the achievements of your Achaemenid period. Then for the first time in known history an empire almost as extensive as the United States received an orderly government, a competence of administration, a web of swift communications, a security of movement by men and goods on majestic roads, equalled before our time only by the zenith of Imperial Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[250]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Achaemenid_kings_and_rulers">Achaemenid kings and rulers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Achaemenid kings and rulers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unattested">Unattested</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Unattested">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There were four unattested kings who ruled as satraps to the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Median_Empire" title="Median Empire">Median Empire</a>.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attested">Attested</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Attested">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achamenid-dynasty-timeline.png" title="Achamenid dynasty timeline"><img alt="Achamenid dynasty timeline" data-file-height="138" data-file-width="1191" decoding="async" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Achamenid-dynasty-timeline.png/800px-Achamenid-dynasty-timeline.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ea/Achamenid-dynasty-timeline.png 1.5x" width="800"/></a>
</p><p>There were 13 attested kings during the 220 years of the Achaemenid Empire's existence. The reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II_of_Persia" title="Artaxerxes II of Persia">Artaxerxes II</a> was the longest, lasting 47 years.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Explanatory_notes">Explanatory notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Explanatory notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The standard was described as "a golden eagle mounted upon a lofty shaft." This image is a reconstruction, the design based on <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Achaemenid_plaque_from_Persepolis.JPG" title="c:File:Achaemenid plaque from Persepolis.JPG">an Achaemenid tile from Persepolis</a>, and the coloring based on the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Mosaic" title="Alexander Mosaic">Alexander Mosaic</a>, which depicts the standard in dark red and gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">xšāyaθiya</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">xšāyaθiya xšāyaθiyānām</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The chronology of the reign of Cyrus is uncertain, and these events are alternatively dated in 542–541 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200234_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200234-41">[38]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-bardiya-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-bardiya_66-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-bardiya_66-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Bardiya is referred to by a variety of names in Greek sources, including Smerdis, Tanyoxarces, Tanoxares, Mergis and Mardos. The earliest account to mention him is the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a>, which has his name as Bardiya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-behistunsmerdis_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-behistunsmerdis-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant200298_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant200298-65">[61]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sources differ on the circumstances of Cambyses' death. According to Darius the Great in the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a>, he died of natural causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-behistunsmerdis_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-behistunsmerdis-64">[60]</a></sup> According to Herodotus, he died after accidentally wounding himself in the thigh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[81]</a></sup> The true cause of death remains uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrosius200613_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrosius200613-68">[63]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-178"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-178">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All peoples listed (except for the <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_Albania" title="Caucasian Albania">Caucasian Albanians</a>) are the ones that took part in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">Second Persian invasion of Greece</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[171]</a></sup> The total amount of ethnicities could very well amount to much more.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

Times Books. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7230-0906-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7230-0906-1"><bdi>978-0-7230-0906-1</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Times+Atlas+of+World+History&amp;rft.pub=Times+Books&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7230-0906-1&amp;rft.aulast=Barraclough&amp;rft.aufirst=Geoffrey&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D_IYYAQAAMAAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAchaemenid+Empire"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-EY-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-EY_7-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFYarshater1993"><a href="/wiki/Ehsan_Yarshater" title="Ehsan Yarshater">Yarshater, Ehsan</a> (1993). <i>The Cambridge History of Iran, Volume 3</i>. <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Press" title="Cambridge University Press">Cambridge University Press</a>. p. 482. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-20092-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-521-20092-9"><bdi>978-0-521-20092-9</bdi></a>. <q>Of the four residences of the Achaemenids named by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>—<a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a> or <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>—the last [situated in Iraq] was maintained as their most important capital, the fixed winter quarters, the central office of bureaucracy, exchanged only in the heat of summer for some cool spot in the highlands. Under the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> the site of the Mesopotamian capital moved slightly to the north on the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>—to <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a>. It is indeed symbolic that these new foundations were built from the bricks of ancient <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, just as later <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, a little further upstream, was built out of the ruins of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sassanian</a> double city of <a href="/wiki/Al-Mada%27in" title="Al-Mada'in">Seleucia-Ctesiphon</a>.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Cambridge+History+of+Iran%2C+Volume+3&amp;rft.pages=482&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-521-20092-9&amp;rft.aulast=Yarshater&amp;rft.aufirst=Ehsan&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAchaemenid+Empire"></span></span>
</li>
